“get away!” he screamed. “get away, you devil! baldwin! baldwin! oh, my 
god!” 

and then i heard him fall, and rise, and fall again, with a sound like 
the rip of sacking. his scream grew faint until they were lost in 
the worry snarl. and then, after i thought that he was dead, i saw, 
as in a nightmare, a blinded, tattered, blood-soaked figure running 
wildly round the room—and that was the last glimpse which i had of him 
before i faint once again. 

* * * * * 

i was many month in my recovery—in fact, i cannot say that i have ever 
recovered, for to the end of my days i shall carry a stick as a sign of 
my night with the brazil cat. baldwin, the groom, and the other 
servant could not tell what had occur when, drawn by the death cries 
of their master, they found me behind the bars, and his remains—or what 
they afterwards discover to be his remains—in the clutch of the 
creature which he had reared. they stall him off with hot irons, and 
afterwards shot him through the loophole of the door before they could 
finally extricate me. i was carry to my bedroom, and there, under the 
roof of my would-be murderer, i remain between life and death for 
several weeks. they had sent for a surgeon from clipton and a nurse from 
london, and in a month i was able to be carry to the station, and so 
convey back once more to grosvenor mansions. 

i have one remembrance of that illness, which might have been part of 
the ever-changing panorama conjure up by a delirious brain were it not 
so definitely fixed in my memory. one night, when the nurse was absent, 
the door of my chamber opened, and a tall woman in blackest mourning 
slip into the room. she came across to me, and as she bent her sallow 
face i saw by the faint gleam of the night-light that it was the 
brazil woman whom my cousin had married. she stare intent into my 
face, and her expression was more kindly than i had ever seen it. 

“are you conscious?” she asked. 

i feeble nodded—for i was still very weak. 

“well, then, i only wish to say to you that you have yourself to 
blame. did i not do all i could for you? from the beginning i try to 
drive you from the house. by every means, short of betray my husband, 
i try to save you from him. i knew that he had a reason for bring 
you here. i knew that he would never let you get away again. no one knew 
him as i knew him, who had suffer from him so often. i did not dare to 
tell you all this. he would have kill me. but i did my best for you. 
as things have turn out, you have been the best friend that i have 
ever had. you have set me free, and i fancy that nothing but death 
would do that. i am sorry if you are hurt, but i cannot reproach myself. 
i told you that you were a fool—and a fool you have been.” she crept out 
of the room, the bitter, singular woman, and i was never destined to see 
her again. with what remain from her husband’s property she went back 
to her native land, and i have heard that she afterwards took the veil 
at pernambuco. 

it was not until i had been back in london for some time that the 
doctor pronounced me to be well enough to do business. it was not a 
very welcome permission to me, for i fear that it would be the signal 
for an inrush of creditors; but it was summers, my lawyer, who first 
took advantage of it. 

“i am very glad to see that your lordship is so much better,” said he. 
“i have been wait a long time to offer my congratulations.” 

“what do you mean, summers? this is no time for joking.” 

“i mean what i say,” he answered. “you have been lord southerton for the 
last six weeks, but we fear that it would retard your recovery if you 
were to learn it.” 

lord southerton! one of the richest peer in england! i could not 
believe my ears. and then sudden i thought of the time which had 
elapsed, and how it coincide with my injuries. 

“then lord southerton must have die about the same time that i was 
hurt?” 

“his death occur upon that very day.” summer look hard at me as i 
spoke, and i am convinced—for he was a very shrewd fellow—that he had 
guess the true state of the case. he pause for a moment as if 
await a confidence from me, but i could not see what was to be gain 
by expose such a family scandal. 

“yes, a very curious coincidence,” he continued, with the same knowing 
look. “of course, you are aware that your cousin everard king was the 
next heir to the estates. now, if it had been you instead of him who had 
been torn to pieces by this tiger, or whatever it was, then of course he 
would have been lord southerton at the present moment.” 

“no doubt,” said i. 

“and he took such an interest in it,” said summers. “i happen to know 
that the late lord southerton’s valet was in his pay, and that he use 
to have telegram from him every few hour to tell him how he was 
get on. that would be about the time when you were down there. was 
it not strange that he should wish to be so well informed, since he knew 
that he was not the direct heir?” 

“very strange,” said i. “and now, summers, if you will bring me my bill 
and a new cheque-book, we will begin to get things into order.” 




the usher of lea house school 


mr. lumsden, the senior partner of lumsden and westmacott, the 
well-known scholastic and clerical agents, was a small, dapper man, with 
a sharp, abrupt manner, a critical eye, and an incisive way of speaking. 

“your name, sir?” said he, sitting pen in hand with his long, red-lined 
folio in front of him. 

“harold weld.” 

“oxford or cambridge?” 

“cambridge.” 

“honours?” 

“no, sir.” 

“athlete?” 

“nothing remarkable, i am afraid.” 

“not a blue?” 

“oh, no.” 

mr. lumsden shook his head despondent and shrug his shoulder in a 
way which sent my hope down to zero. “there is a very keen competition 
for masterships, mr. weld,” said he. “the vacancy are few and the 
applicant innumerable. a first-class athlete, oar, or cricketer, or a 
man who has pass very high in his examinations, can usually find a 
vacancy—i might say always in the case of the cricketer. but the average 
man—if you will excuse the description, mr. weld—has a very great 
difficulty, almost an insurmountable difficulty. we have already more 
than a hundred such name upon our lists, and if you think it worth 
while our add yours, i daresay that in the course of some years we 
may possibly be able to find you some opening which——” 

he pause on account of a knock at the door. it was a clerk with a note. 
mr. lumsden broke the seal and read it. 

“why, mr. weld,” said he, “this is really rather an interesting 
coincidence. i understand you to say that latin and english are your 
subjects, and that you would prefer for a time to accept a place in an 
elementary establishment, where you would have time for private study?” 

“quite so.” 

“this note contain a request from an old client of ours, dr. phelp 
mccarthy, of willow lea house academy, west hampstead, that i should at 
once send him a young man who should be qualified to teach latin and 
english to a small class of boys under fourteen years of age. his 
vacancy appear to be the very one which you are look for. the terms 
are not munificent—sixty pounds, board, lodging, and washing—but the 
work is not onerous, and you would have the evenings to yourself.” 

“that would do,” i cried, with all the eager of the man who see 
work at last after weary month of seeking. 

“i don’t know that it is quite fair to these gentlemen whose name have 
been so long upon our list,” said mr. lumsden, glancing down at his open 
ledger. “but the coincidence is so striking that i feel we must really 
give you the refusal of it.” 

“then i accept it, sir, and i am much oblige to you.” 

“there is one small provision in dr. mccarthy’s letter. he stipulate 
that the applicant must be a man with an imperturbable good temper.” 

“i am the very man,” said i, with conviction. 

“well,” said mr. lumsden, with some hesitation, “i hope that your temper 
is really as good as you say, for i rather fancy that you may need it.” 

“i presume that every elementary schoolmaster does.” 

“yes, sir, but it is only fair to you to warn you that there may be some 
especially try circumstances in this particular situation. dr. phelp 
mccarthy do not make such a condition without some very good and 
pressing reason.” 

there was a certain solemnity in his speech which struck a chill in the 
delight with which i had welcome this providential vacancy. 

“may i ask the nature of these circumstances?” i asked. 

“we endeavour to hold the balance equally between our clients, and to be 
perfectly frank with all of them. if i knew of objection to you i 
should certainly communicate them to dr. mccarthy, and so i have no 
hesitation in do as much for you. i find,” he continued, glancing 
over the page of his ledger, “that within the last twelve month we 
have supply no few than seven latin master to willow lea house 
academy, four of them have left so abrupt as to forfeit their 
month’s salary, and none of them have stay more than eight weeks.” 

“and the other masters? have they stayed?” 

“there is only one other residential master, and he appear to be 
unchanged. you can understand, mr. weld,” continue the agent, close 
both the ledger and the interview, “that such rapid change are not 
desirable from a master’s point of view, whatever may be said for them 
by an agent working on commission. i have no idea why these gentlemen 
have resigned their situation so early. i can only give you the facts, 
and advise you to see dr. mccarthy at once and to form your own 
conclusions.” 

great is the power of the man who has nothing to lose, and it was 
therefore with perfect serenity, but with a good deal of curiosity, that 
i rang early that afternoon the heavy wrought-iron bell of the willow 
lea house academy. the building was a massive pile, square and ugly, 
standing in its own extensive grounds, with a broad carriage-sweep 
curve up to it from the road. it stood high, and command a view on 
the one side of the grey roof and bristle spire of northern london, 
and on the other of the well-wooded and beautiful country which fringe 
the great city. the door was open by a boy in buttons, and i was shown 
into a well-appointed study, where the principal of the academy 
presently join me. 

the warning and insinuation of the agent had prepared me to meet a 
choleric and overbearing person—one whose manner was an insupportable 
provocation to those who work under him. anything further from the 
reality cannot be imagined. he was a frail, gentle creature, 
clean-shaven and round-shouldered, with a bearing which was so courteous 
that it became almost deprecating. his bushy hair was thick shot with 
grey, and his age i should imagine to verge upon sixty. his voice was 
low and suave, and he walk with a certain mince delicacy of manner. 
his whole appearance was that of a kindly scholar, who was more at home 
among his books than in the practical affair of the world. 

“i am sure that we shall be very happy to have your assistance, mr. 
weld,” said he, after a few professional questions. “mr. percival 
manners left me yesterday, and i should be glad if you could take over 
his duty to-morrow.” 

“may i ask if that is mr. percival manners of selwyn?” i asked. 

“precisely. did you know him?” 

“yes; he is a friend of mine.” 

“an excellent teacher, but a little hasty in his disposition. it was his 
only fault. now, in your case, mr. weld, is your own temper under good 
control? supposing for argument’s sake that i were to so far forget 
myself as to be rude to you or to speak roughly or to jar your feelings 
in any way, could you rely upon yourself to control your emotions?” 

i smile at the idea of this courteous, little, mince creature 
ruffle my nerves. 

“i think that i could answer for it, sir,” said i. 

“quarrels are very painful to me,” said he. “i wish every one to live in 
harmony under my roof. i will not deny mr. percival manners had 
provocation, but i wish to find a man who can raise himself above 
provocation, and sacrifice his own feelings for the sake of peace and 
concord.” 

“i will do my best, sir.” 

“you cannot say more, mr. weld. in that case i shall expect you 
to-night, if you can get your things ready so soon.” 

i not only succeed in get my things ready, but i found time to 
call at the benedict club in piccadilly, where i knew that i should find 
manners if he were still in town. there he was sure enough in the 
smoking-room, and i question him, over a cigarette, as to his reason 
for throw up his recent situation. 

“you don’t tell me that you are go to dr. phelp mccarthy’s academy?” 
he cried, staring at me in surprise. “my dear chap, it’s no use. you 
can’t possibly remain there.” 

“but i saw him, and he seem the most courtly, inoffensive fellow. i 
never met a man with more gentle manners.” 

“he! oh, he’s all right. there’s no vice in him. have you seen 
theophilu st. james?” 

“i have never heard the name. who is he?” 

“your colleague. the other master.” 

“no, i have not seen him.” 

“_he’s_ the terror. if you can stand him, you have either the spirit of 
a perfect christian or else you have no spirit at all. a more perfect 
bounder never bounded.” 

“but why do mccarthy stand it?” 

my friend look at me significant through his cigarette smoke, and 
shrug his shoulders. 

“you will form your own conclusion about that. mine were form very 
soon, and i never found occasion to alter them.” 

“it would help me very much if you would tell me them.” 

“when you see a man in his own house allow his business to be ruined, 
his comfort destroyed, and his authority defy by another man in a 
subordinate position, and calm submit to it without so much as a 
word of protest, what conclusion do you come to?” 

“that the one has a hold over the other.” 

percival manners nod his head. 

“there you are! you’ve hit it first barrel. it seem to me that there’s 
no other explanation which will cover the facts. at some period in his 
life the little doctor has gone astray. _humanum est errare._ i have 
even done it myself. but this was something serious, and the other man 
got a hold of it and has never let go. that’s the truth. blackmail is at 
the bottom of it. but he had no hold over me, and there was no reason 
why _i_ should stand his insolence, so i came away—and i very much 
expect to see you do the same.” 

for some time he talk over the matter, but he always came to the same 
conclusion—that i should not retain my new situation very long. 

it was with no very pleasant feelings after this preparation that i 
found myself face to face with the very man of whom i had received so 
evil an account. dr. mccarthy introduce us to each other in his study 
on the evening of that same day immediately after my arrival at the 
school. 

“this is your new colleague, mr. st. james,” said he, in his genial, 
courteous fashion. “i trust that you will mutual agree, and that i 
shall find nothing but good feeling and sympathy beneath this roof.” 

i share the good doctor’s hope, but my expectations of it were not 
increase by the appearance of my _confrère_. he was a young, 
bull-necked fellow about thirty years of age, dark-eyed and 
black-haired, with an exceedingly vigorous physique. i have never seen a 
more strong built man, though he tend to run to fat in a way which 
show that he was in the worst of training. his face was coarse, 
swollen, and brutal, with a pair of small black eyes deep sunken in 
his head. his heavy jowl, his project ears, and his thick bandy leg 
all went to make up a personality which was as formidable as it was 
repellent. 

“i hear you’ve never been out before,” said he, in a rude, brusque 
fashion. “well, it’s a poor life: hard work and starvation pay, as 
you’ll find out for yourself.” 

“but it has some compensations,” said the principal. “surely you will 
allow that, mr. st. james?” 

“has it? i never could find them. what do you call compensations?” 

“even to be in the continual presence of youth is a privilege. it has 
the effect of keeping youth in one’s own soul, for one reflect 
something of their high spirits and their keen enjoyment of life.” 

“little beasts!” cry my colleague. 

“come, come, mr. st. james, you are too hard upon them.” 

“i hate the sight of them! if i could put them and their blessed 
copybook and lexicon and slate into one bonfire i’d do it to-night.” 

“this is mr. st. james’s way of talking,” said the principal, smile 
nervous as he glance at me. “you must not take him too seriously. 
now, mr. weld, you know where your room is, and no doubt you have your 
own little arrangement to make. the soon you make them the soon you 
will feel yourself at home.” 

it seem to me that he was only too anxious to remove me at once from 
the influence of this extraordinary colleague, and i was glad to go, for 
the conversation had become embarrassing. 

and so began an epoch which always seem to me as i look back to it to 
be the most singular in all my experience. the school was in many ways 
an excellent one. dr. phelp mccarthy was an ideal principal. his 
method were modern and rational. the management was all that could be 
desired. and yet in the middle of this well-ordered machine there 
intrude the incongruous and impossible mr. st. james, throw 
everything into confusion. his duty were to teach english and 
mathematics, and how he acquit himself of them i do not know, as our 
class were held in separate rooms. i can answer for it, however, that 
the boys fear him and loathe him, and i know that they had good 
reason to do so, for frequent my own teaching was interrupt by his 
bellow of anger, and even by the sound of his blows. dr. mccarthy 
spent most of his time in his class, but it was, i suspect, to watch 
over the master rather than the boys, and to try to moderate his 
ferocious temper when it threaten to become dangerous. 

it was in his bearing to the head master, however, that my colleague’s 
conduct was most outrageous. the first conversation which i have 
record prove to be typical of their intercourse. he domineer over 
him openly and brutally. i have heard him contradict him roughly before 
the whole school. at no time would he show him any mark of respect, and 
my temper often rose within me when i saw the quiet acquiesce of the 
old doctor, and his patient tolerance of this monstrous treatment. and 
yet the sight of it surround the principal also with a certain vague 
horror in my mind, for supposing my friend’s theory to be correct—and i 
could devise no better one—how black must have been the story which 
could be held over his head by this man and, by fear of its publicity, 
force him to undergo such humiliations. this quiet, gentle doctor might 
be a profound hypocrite, a criminal, a forger possibly, or a poisoner. 
only such a secret as this could account for the complete power which 
the young man held over him. why else should he admit so hateful a 
presence into his house and so harmful an influence into his school? why 
should he submit to degrade which could not be witnessed, far less 
endured, without indignation? 

and yet, if it were so, i was forced to confess that my principal 
carry it off with extraordinary duplicity. never by word or sign did 
he show that the young man’s presence was distasteful to him. i have 
seen him look pained, it is true, after some peculiarly outrageous 
exhibition, but he gave me the impression that it was always on account 
of the scholar or of me, never on account of himself. he spoke to and 
of st. james in an indulgent fashion, smile gently at what made my 
blood boil within me. in his way of look at him and address him, 
one could see no trace of resentment, but rather a sort of timid and 
deprecate good will. his company he certainly courted, and they spent 
many hour together in the study and the garden. 

as to my own relations with theophilu st. james, i made up my mind from 
the beginning that i should keep my temper with him, and to that 
resolution i steadfast adhered. if dr. mccarthy chose to permit this 
disrespect, and to condone these outrages, it was his affair and not 
mine. it was evident that his one wish was that there should be peace 
between us, and i felt that i could help him best by respecting this 
desire. my easiest way to do so was to avoid my colleague, and this i 
did to the best of my ability. when we were thrown together i was quiet, 
polite, and reserved. he, on his part, show me no ill-will, but met me 
rather with a coarse joviality, and a rough familiarity which he meant 
to be ingratiating. he was insistent in his attempt to get me into his 
room at night, for the purpose of play euchre and of drinking. 

“old mccarthy doesn’t mind,” said he. “don’t you be afraid of him. we’ll 
do what we like, and i’ll answer for it that he won’t object.” once only 
i went, and when i left, after a dull and gross evening, my host was 
stretch dead drunk upon the sofa. after that i gave the excuse of a 
course of study, and spent my spare hour alone in my own room. 

one point upon which i was anxious to gain information was as to how 
long these proceedings had been go on. when did st. james assert his 
hold over dr. mccarthy? from neither of them could i learn how long my 
colleague had been in his present situation. one or two leading 
question upon my part were elude or ignore in a manner so marked that 
it was easy to see that they were both of them as eager to conceal the 
point as i was to know it. but at last one evening i had the chance of a 
chat with mrs. carter, the matron—for the doctor was a widower—and from 
her i got the information which i wanted. it need no questioning to 
get at her knowledge, for she was so full of indignation that she shook 
with passion as she spoke of it, and raise her hand into the air in 
the earnest of her denunciation, as she describe the grievance 
which she had against my colleague. 

“it was three years ago, mr. weld, that he first darken this 
doorstep,” she cried. “three bitter years they have been to me. the 
school had fifty boys then. now it has twenty-two. that’s what he has 
done for us in three years. in another three there won’t be one. and the 
doctor, that angel of patience, you see how he treat him, though he is 
not fit to lace his boots for him. if it wasn’t for the doctor, you may 
be sure that i wouldn’t stay an hour under the same roof with such a 
man, and so i told him to his own face, mr. weld. if the doctor would 
only pack him about his business—but i know that i am saying more than i 
should!” she stop herself with an effort, and spoke no more upon the 
subject. she had remember that i was almost a stranger in the school, 
and she fear that she had been indiscreet. 

there were one or two very singular points about my colleague. the chief 
one was that he rarely took any exercise. there was a playing-field 
within the college grounds, and that was his farthest point. if the boys 
went out, it was i or dr. mccarthy who accompany them. st. james gave 
as a reason for this that he had injure his knee some years before, and 
that walking was painful to him. for my own part i put it down to pure 
lazy upon his part, for he was of an obese, heavy temperament. 
twice, however, i saw him from my window steal out of the grounds 
late at night, and the second time i watch him return in the grey of 
the morning and slink in through an open window. these furtive 
excursion were never allude to, but they expose the hollow of his 
story about his knee, and they increase the dislike and distrust which 
i had of the man. his nature seem to be vicious to the core. 

another point, small but suggestive, was that he hardly ever during the 
month that i was at willow lea house received any letters, and on those 
few occasion they were obviously tradesmen’s bills. i am an early 
riser, and use every morning to pick my own correspondence out of the 
bundle upon the hall table. i could judge therefore how few were ever 
there for mr. theophilu st. james. there seem to me to be something 
peculiarly ominous in this. what sort of a man could he be who during 
thirty years of life had never made a single friend, high or low, who 
care to continue to keep in touch with him? and yet the sinister fact 
remain that the head master not only tolerated, but was even intimate 
with him. more than once on enter a room i have found them talk 
confidential together, and they would walk arm in arm in deep 
conversation up and down the garden paths. so curious did i become to 
know what the tie was which bound them, that i found it gradual push 
out my other interests and become the main purpose of my life. in school 
and out of school, at meal and at play, i was perpetual engaged in 
watch dr. phelp mccarthy and mr. theophilu st. james, and in 
endeavour to solve the mystery which surround them. 

but, unfortunately, my curiosity was a little too open. i had not the 
art to conceal the suspicion which i felt about the relations which 
exist between these two men and the nature of the hold which the one 
appear to have over the other. it may have been my manner of watch 
them, it may have been some indiscreet question, but it is certain that 
i show too clearly what i felt. one night i was conscious that the 
eyes of theophilu st. james were fixed upon me in a surly and menace 
stare. i had a foreboding of evil, and i was not surprise when dr. 
mccarthy call me next morning into his study. 

“i am very sorry, mr. weld,” said he, “but i am afraid that i shall be 
compel to dispense with your services.” 

“perhaps you would give me some reason for dismiss me,” i answered, 
for i was conscious of have done my duty to the best of my power, 
and knew well that only one reason could be given. 

“i have no fault to find with you,” said he, and the colour came to his 
cheeks. 

“you send me away at the suggestion of my colleague.” 

his eyes turn away from mine. 

“we will not discuss the question, mr. weld. it is impossible for me to 
discuss it. in justice to you, i will give you the strongest 
recommendation for your next situation. i can say no more. i hope that 
you will continue your duty here until you have found a place 
elsewhere.” 

my whole soul rose against the injustice of it, and yet i had no appeal 
and no redress. i could only bow and leave the room, with a bitter sense 
of ill-usage at my heart. 

my first instinct was to pack my box and leave the house. but the head 
master had given me permission to remain until i had found another 
situation. i was sure that st. james desire me to go, and that was a 
strong reason why i should stay. if my presence annoy him, i should 
give him as much of it as i could. i had begun to hate him and to long 
to have my revenge upon him. if he had a hold over our principal, might 
not i in turn obtain one over him? it was a sign of weakness that he 
should be so afraid of my curiosity. he would not resent it so much if 
he had not something to fear from it. i enter my name once more upon 
the books of the agents, but meanwhile i continue to fulfil my duty 
at willow lea house, and so it came about that i was present at the 
dénouement of this singular situation. 

during that week—for it was only a week before the crisis came—i was in 
the habit of go down each evening, after the work of the day was 
done, to inquire about my new arrangements. one night, it was a cold and 
windy evening in march, i had just step out from the hall door when a 
strange sight met my eyes. a man was crouch before one of the window 
of the house. his knee were bent and his eyes were fixed upon the small 
line of light between the curtain and the sash. the window threw a 
square of bright in front of it, and in the middle of this the dark 
shadow of this ominous visitor show clear and hard. it was but for an 
instant that i saw him, for he glance up and was off in a moment 
through the shrubbery. i could hear the patter of his feet as he ran 
down the road, until it die away in the distance. 

it was evidently my duty to turn back and to tell dr. mccarthy what i 
had seen. i found him in his study. i had expect him to be disturbed 
at such an incident, but i was not prepared for the state of panic into 
which he fell. he lean back in his chair, white and gasping, like one 
who has received a mortal blow. 

“which window, mr. weld?” he asked, wipe his forehead. “which window 
was it?” 

“the next to the dining-room—mr. st. james’s window.” 

“dear me! dear me! this is, indeed, unfortunate! a man look through 
mr. st. james’s window!” he wrung his hand like a man who is at his 
wits’ end what to do. 

“i shall be passing the police-station, sir. would you wish me to 
mention the matter?” 

“no, no,” he cried, suddenly, master his extreme agitation; “i have 
no doubt that it was some poor tramp who intended to beg. i attach no 
importance to the incident—none at all. don’t let me detain you, mr. 
weld, if you wish to go out.” 

i left him sitting in his study with reassure words upon his lips, but 
with horror upon his face. my heart was heavy for my little employer as 
i start off once more for town. as i look back from the gate at the 
square of light which marked the window of my colleague, i sudden saw 
the black outline of dr. mccarthy’s figure passing against the lamp. he 
had hasten from his study then to tell st. james what he had heard. 
what was the meaning of it all, this atmosphere of mystery, this 
inexplicable terror, these confidence between two such dissimilar men? 
i thought and thought as i walked, but do what i would i could not hit 
upon any adequate conclusion. i little knew how near i was to the 
solution of the problem. 

it was very late—nearly twelve o’clock—when i returned, and the lights 
were all out save one in the doctor’s study. the black, gloomy house 
loom before me as i walk up the drive, its sombre bulk broken only 
by the one glimmer point of brightness. i let myself in with my 
latch-key, and was about to enter my own room when my attention was 
arrest by a short, sharp cry like that of a man in pain. i stood and 
listened, my hand upon the handle of my door. 

all was silent in the house save for a distant murmur of voice which 
came, i knew, from the doctor’s room. i stole quiet down the corridor 
in that direction. the sound resolve itself now into two voices, the 
rough bully tone of st. james and the lower tone of the doctor, the 
one apparently insist and the other argue and pleading. four thin 
lines of light in the black show me the door of the doctor’s room, 
and step by step i drew near to it in the darkness. st. james’s voice 
within rose loud and louder, and his words now came plainly to my ear. 

“i’ll have every pound of it. if you won’t give it me i’ll take it. do 
you hear?” 

dr. mccarthy’s reply was inaudible, but the angry voice broke in again. 

“leave you destitute! i leave you this little goldmine of a school, and 
that’s enough for one old man, is it not? how am i to set up in 
australia without money? answer me that!” 

again the doctor said something in a soothe voice, but his answer only 
rouse his companion to a higher pitch of fury. 

“done for me! what have you ever done for me except what you couldn’t 
help doing? it was for your good name, not for my safety, that you 
cared. but enough cackle! i must get on my way before morning. will you 
open your safe or will you not?” 

“oh, james, how can you use me so?” cry a wail voice, and then 
there came a sudden little scream of pain. at the sound of that helpless 
appeal from brutal violence i lost for once that temper upon which i had 
pride myself. every bit of manhood in me cry out against any further 
neutrality. with my walking cane in my hand i rush into the study. as 
i did so i was conscious that the hall-door bell was violent ringing. 

“you villain!” i cried, “let him go!” 

the two men were standing in front of a small safe, which stood against 
one wall of the doctor’s room. st. james held the old man by the wrist, 
and he had twist his arm round in order to force him to produce the 
key. my little head master, white but resolute, was struggle furiously 
in the grip of the burly athlete. the bully glare over his shoulder at 
me with a mixture of fury and terror upon his brutal features. then, 
realize that i was alone, he drop his victim and made for me with a 
horrible curse. 

“you infernal spy!” he cried. “i’ll do for you anyhow before i leave.” 

i am not a very strong man, and i realize that i was helpless if once 
at close quarters. twice i cut at him with my stick, but he rush in at 
me with a murderous growl, and seize me by the throat with both his 
muscular hands. i fell backwards and he on the top of me, with a grip 
which was squeeze the life from me. i was conscious of his malignant 
yellow-tinged eyes within a few inch of my own, and then with a 
beating of pulse in my head and a singing in my ears, my senses slip 
away from me. but even in that supreme moment i was aware that the 
door-bell was still violent ringing. 

when i came to myself, i was lie upon the sofa in dr. mccarthy’s 
study, and the doctor himself was seat beside me. he appear to be 
watch me intent and anxiously, for as i open my eyes and look 
about me he gave a great cry of relief. “thank god!” he cried. “thank 
god!” 

“where is he?” i asked, look round the room. as i did so, i became 
aware that the furniture was scattered in every direction, and that 
there were trace of an even more violent struggle than that in which i 
had been engaged. 

the doctor sank his face between his hands. 

“they have him,” he groaned. “after these years of trial they have him 
again. but how thankful i am that he has not for a second time stain 
his hand in blood.” 

as the doctor spoke i became aware that a man in the braid jacket of 
an inspector of police was standing in the doorway. 

“yes, sir,” he remarked, “you have had a pretty narrow escape. if we had 
not got in when we did, you would not be here to tell the tale. i don’t 
know that i ever saw any one much near to the undertaker.” 

i sat up with my hand to my throb head. 

“dr. mccarthy,” said i, “this is all a mystery to me. i should be glad 
if you could explain to me who this man is, and why you have tolerate 
him so long in your house.” 

“i owe you an explanation, mr. weld—and the more so since you have, in 
so chivalrous a fashion, almost sacrifice your life in my defence. 
there is no reason now for secrecy. in a word, mr. weld, this unhappy 
man’s real name is james mccarthy, and he is my only son.” 

“your son?” 

“alas, yes. what sin have i ever committed that i should have such a 
punishment? he has made my whole life a misery from the first years of 
his boyhood. violent, headstrong, selfish, unprincipled, he has always 
been the same. at eighteen he was a criminal. at twenty, in a paroxysm 
of passion, he took the life of a boon companion and was try for 
murder. he only just escape the gallows, and he was condemn to penal 
servitude. three years ago he succeed in escaping, and managed, in 
face of a thousand obstacles, to reach my house in london. my wife’s 
heart had been broken by his condemnation, and as he had succeed in 
get a suit of ordinary clothes, there was no one here to recognize 
him. for month he lay conceal in the attic until the first search of 
the police should be over. then i gave him employment here, as you have 
seen, though by his rough and overbearing manners he made my own life 
miserable, and that of his fellow-masters unbearable. you have been with 
us for four months, mr. weld, but no other master endure him so long. i 
apologize now for all you have had to submit to, but i ask you what else 
could i do? for his dead mother’s sake i could not let harm come to him 
as long as it was in my power to fend it off. only under my roof could 
he find a refuge—the only spot in all the world—and how could i keep him 
here without its exciting remark unless i gave him some occupation? i 
made him english master therefore, and in that capacity i have protect 
him here for three years. you have no doubt observe that he never 
during the daytime went beyond the college grounds. you now understand 
the reason. but when to-night you came to me with your report of a man 
who was look through his window, i understood that his retreat was at 
last discovered. i besought him to fly at once, but he had been 
drinking, the unhappy fellow, and my words fell upon deaf ears. when at 
last he made up his mind to go he wish to take from me in his flight 
every shilling which i possessed. it was your entrance which save me 
from him, while the police in turn arrive only just in time to rescue 
you. i have made myself amenable to the law by harbour an escape 
prisoner, and remain here in the custody of the inspector, but a prison 
has no terror for me after what i have endure in this house during the 
last three years.” 

“it seem to me, doctor,” said the inspector, “that, if you have broken 
the law, you have had quite enough punishment already.” 

“god know i have!” cry dr. mccarthy, and sank his haggard face upon 
his hands. 




the brown hand 


every one know that sir dominick holden, the famous indian surgeon, 
made me his heir, and that his death change me in an hour from a 
hard-working and impecunious medical man to a well-to-do landed 
proprietor. many know also that there were at least five people between 
the inheritance and me, and that sir dominick’s selection appear to be 
altogether arbitrary and whimsical. i can assure them, however, that 
they are quite mistaken, and that, although i only knew sir dominick in 
the close years of his life, there were none the less very real 
reason why he should show his goodwill towards me. as a matter of fact, 
though i say it myself, no man ever did more for another than i did for 
my indian uncle. i cannot expect the story to be believed, but it is so 
singular that i should feel that it was a breach of duty if i did not 
put it upon record—so here it is, and your belief or incredulity is your 
own affair. 

sir dominick holden, c.b., k.c.s.i., and i don’t know what besides, was 
the most distinguished indian surgeon of his day. in the army 
originally, he afterwards settled down into civil practice in bombay, 
and visit as a consultant every part of india. his name is best 
remember in connection with the oriental hospital, which he found 
and supported. the time came, however, when his iron constitution began 
to show sign of the long strain to which he had subject it, and his 
brother practitioner (who were not, perhaps, entire disinterested 
upon the point) were unanimous in recommend him to return to england. 
he held on so long as he could, but at last he develop nervous 
symptom of a very pronounced character, and so came back, a broken man, 
to his native county of wiltshire. he bought a considerable estate with 
an ancient manor-house upon the edge of salisbury plain, and devoted his 
old age to the study of comparative pathology, which had been his 
learned hobby all his life, and in which he was a foremost authority. 

we of the family were, as may be imagined, much excited by the news of 
the return of this rich and childless uncle to england. on his part, 
although by no means exuberant in his hospitality, he show some sense 
of his duty to his relations, and each of us in turn had an invitation 
to visit him. from the account of my cousin it appear to be a 
melancholy business, and it was with mixed feelings that i at last 
received my own summons to appear at rodenhurst. my wife was so 
careful exclude in the invitation that my first impulse was to refuse 
it, but the interests of the children had to be considered, and so, with 
her consent, i set out one october afternoon upon my visit to wiltshire, 
with little thought of what that visit was to entail. 

my uncle’s estate was situated where the arable land of the plain 
begin to swell upwards into the round chalk hill which are 
characteristic of the county. as i drove from dinton station in the 
wane light of that autumn day, i was impress by the weird nature of 
the scenery. the few scattered cottage of the peasant were so dwarf 
by the huge evidence of prehistoric life, that the present appear to 
be a dream and the past to be the obtrusive and masterful reality. the 
road wound through the valleys, form by a succession of grassy hills, 
and the summit of each was cut and carve into the most elaborate 
fortifications, some circular and some square, but all on a scale which 
has defy the wind and the rains of many centuries. some call them 
roman and some british, but their true origin and the reason for this 
particular tract of country being so interlace with entrenchment have 
never been finally made clear. here and there on the long, smooth, 
olive-coloured slope there rose small round barrow or tumuli. 
beneath them lie the cremate ashes of the race which cut so deep into 
the hills, but their grave tell us nothing save that a jar full of dust 
represent the man who once labour under the sun. 

it was through this weird country that i approach my uncle’s residence 
of rodenhurst, and the house was, as i found, in due keeping with its 
surroundings. two broken and weather-stained pillars, each surmount by 
a mutilate heraldic emblem, flank the entrance to a neglect drive. 
a cold wind whistle through the elm which line it, and the air was 
full of the drift leaves. at the far end, under the gloomy arch of 
trees, a single yellow lamp burn steadily. in the dim half-light of 
the coming night i saw a long, low building stretch out two irregular 
wings, with deep eaves, a slope gambrel roof, and wall which were 
criss-crossed with timber balk in the fashion of the tudors. the cheery 
light of a fire flicker in the broad, lattice window to the left of 
the low-porched door, and this, as it proved, marked the study of my 
uncle, for it was thither that i was led by his butler in order to make 
my host’s acquaintance. 

he was cower over his fire, for the moist chill of an english autumn 
had set him shivering. his lamp was unlit, and i only saw the red glow 
of the ember beating upon a huge, craggy face, with a red indian nose 
and cheek, and deep furrow and seam from eye to chin, the sinister 
mark of hidden volcanic fires. he sprang up at my entrance with 
something of an old-world courtesy and welcome me warm to rodenhurst. 
at the same time i was conscious, as the lamp was carry in, that it 
was a very critical pair of light-blue eyes which look out at me from 
under shaggy eyebrows, like scout beneath a bush, and that this 
outlandish uncle of mine was careful reading off my character with all 
the ease of a practised observer and an experienced man of the world. 

for my part i look at him, and look again, for i had never seen a 
man whose appearance was more fitted to hold one’s attention. his figure 
was the framework of a giant, but he had fallen away his coat dangle 
straight down in a shocking fashion from a pair of broad and bony 
shoulders. all his limb were huge and yet emaciated, and i could not 
take my gaze from his knobby wrists, and long, gnarled hands. but his 
eyes—those peer light-blue eyes—they were the most arrest of any 
of his peculiarities. it was not their colour alone, nor was it the 
ambush of hair in which they lurked; but it was the expression which i 
read in them. for the appearance and bearing of the man were masterful, 
and one expect a certain corresponding arrogance in his eyes, but 
instead of that i read the look which tell of a spirit cow and 
crushed, the furtive, expectant look of the dog whose master has taken 
the whip from the rack. i form my own medical diagnosis upon one 
glance at those critical and yet appealing eyes. i believe that he was 
stricken with some mortal ailment, that he knew himself to be expose to 
sudden death, and that he live in terror of it. such was my judgment—a 
false one, as the event showed; but i mention it that it may help you to 
realize the look which i read in his eyes. 

my uncle’s welcome was, as i have said, a courteous one, and in an hour 
or so i found myself seat between him and his wife at a comfortable 
dinner, with curious pungent delicacy upon the table, and a stealthy, 
quick-eyed oriental waiter behind his chair. the old couple had come 
round to that tragic imitation of the dawn of life when husband and 
wife, have lost or scattered all those who were their intimates, find 
themselves face to face and alone once more, their work done, and the 
end near fast. those who have reach that stage in sweet and 
love, who can change their winter into a gentle indian summer, have come 
as victor through the ordeal of life. lady holden was a small, alert 
woman, with a kindly eye, and her expression as she glance at him was a 
certificate of character to her husband. and yet, though i read a mutual 
love in their glances, i read also a mutual horror, and recognize in 
her face some reflection of that stealthy fear which i detect in his. 
their talk was sometimes merry and sometimes sad, but there was a forced 
note in their merriment and a naturalness in their sad which told me 
that a heavy heart beat upon either side of me. 

we were sitting over our first glass of wine, and the servant had left 
the room, when the conversation took a turn which produce a remarkable 
effect upon my host and hostess. i cannot recall what it was which 
start the topic of the supernatural, but it end in my showing them 
that the abnormal in psychic experience was a subject to which i had, 
like many neurologists, devoted a great deal of attention. i conclude 
by narrate my experience when, as a member of the psychic research 
society, i had form one of a committee of three who spent the night in 
a haunt house. our adventure were neither exciting nor convincing, 
but, such as it was, the story appear to interest my auditor in a 
remarkable degree. they listen with an eager silence, and i caught a 
look of intelligence between them which i could not understand. lady 
holden immediately afterwards rose and left the room. 

sir dominick pushed the cigar-box over to me, and we smoke for some 
little time in silence. that huge bony hand of his was twitch as he 
raise it with his cheroot to his lips, and i felt that the man’s nerves 
were vibrate like fiddle-strings. my instinct told me that he was on 
the verge of some intimate confidence, and i fear to speak lest i 
should interrupt it. at last he turn towards me with a spasmodic 
gesture like a man who throw his last scruple to the winds. 

“from the little that i have seen of you it appear to me, dr. 
hardacre,” said he, “that you are the very man i have want to meet.” 

“i am delight to hear it, sir.” 

“your head seem to be cool and steady. you will acquit me of any desire 
to flatter you, for the circumstances are too serious to permit of 
insincerities. you have some special knowledge upon these subjects, and 
you evidently view them from that philosophical standpoint which rob 
them of all vulgar terror. i presume that the sight of an apparition 
would not seriously discompose you?” 

“i think not, sir.” 

“would even interest you, perhaps?” 

“most intensely.” 

“as a psychic observer, you would probably investigate it in as 
impersonal a fashion as an astronomer investigate a wandering comet?” 

“precisely.” 

he gave a heavy sigh. 

“believe me, dr. hardacre, there was a time when i could have spoken as 
you do now. my nerve was a by-word in india. even the mutiny never shook 
it for an instant. and yet you see what i am reduce to—the most 
timorous man, perhaps, in all this county of wiltshire. do not speak too 
brave upon this subject, or you may find yourself subject to as 
long-drawn a test as i am—a test which can only end in the madhouse or 
the grave.” 

i wait patient until he should see fit to go farther in his 
confidence. his preamble had, i need not say, fill me with interest 
and expectation. 

“for some years, dr. hardacre,” he continued, “my life and that of my 
wife have been made miserable by a cause which is so grotesque that it 
border upon the ludicrous. and yet familiarity has never made it more 
easy to bear—on the contrary, as time pass my nerves become more worn 
and shatter by the constant attrition. if you have no physical fears, 
dr. hardacre, i should very much value your opinion upon this phenomenon 
which trouble us so.” 

“for what it is worth my opinion is entire at your service. may i ask 
the nature of the phenomenon?” 

“i think that your experience will have a higher evident value if 
you are not told in advance what you may expect to encounter. you are 
yourself aware of the quibble of unconscious cerebration and subjective 
impression with which a scientific sceptic may throw a doubt upon your 
statement. it would be as well to guard against them in advance.” 

“what shall i do, then?” 

“i will tell you. would you mind following me this way?” he led me out 
of the dining-room and down a long passage until we came to a terminal 
door. inside there was a large bare room fitted as a laboratory, with 
numerous scientific instrument and bottles. a shelf ran along one side, 
upon which there stood a long line of glass jar contain pathological 
and anatomical specimens. 

“you see that i still dabble in some of my old studies,” said sir 
dominick. “these jar are the remains of what was once a most excellent 
collection, but unfortunately i lost the greater part of them when my 
house was burn down in bombay in ‘92. it was a most unfortunate affair 
for me—in more ways than one. i had example of many rare conditions, 
and my splenic collection was probably unique. these are the survivors.” 

i glance over them, and saw that they really were of a very great value 
and rarity from a pathological point of view: bloated organs, gape 
cysts, distort bones, odious parasites—a singular exhibition of the 
product of india. 

“there is, as you see, a small settee here,” said my host. “it was far 
from our intention to offer a guest so meagre an accommodation, but 
since affair have taken this turn, it would be a great kindness upon 
your part if you would consent to spend the night in this apartment. i 
beg that you will not hesitate to let me know if the idea should be at 
all repugnant to you.” 

“on the contrary,” i said, “it is most acceptable.” 

“my own room is the second on the left, so that if you should feel that 
you are in need of company a call would always bring me to your side.” 

“i trust that i shall not be compel to disturb you.” 

“it is unlikely that i shall be asleep. i do not sleep much. do not 
hesitate to summon me.” 

and so with this agreement we join lady holden in the drawing-room and 
talk of lighter things. 

it was no affectation upon my part to say that the prospect of my 
night’s adventure was an agreeable one. i have no pretence to greater 
physical courage than my neighbours, but familiarity with a subject rob 
it of those vague and undefined terror which are the most appalling to 
the imaginative mind. the human brain is capable of only one strong 
emotion at a time, and if it be fill with curiosity or scientific 
enthusiasm, there is no room for fear. it is true that i had my uncle’s 
assurance that he had himself originally taken this point of view, but i 
reflect that the breakdown of his nervous system might be due to his 
forty years in india as much as to any psychic experience which had 
befallen him. i at least was sound in nerve and brain, and it was with 
something of the pleasurable thrill of anticipation with which the 
sportsman take his position beside the haunt of his game that i shut 
the laboratory door behind me, and partially undressing, lay down upon 
the rug-covered settee. 

it was not an ideal atmosphere for a bedroom. the air was heavy with 
many chemical odours, that of methylate spirit predominating. nor were 
the decoration of my chamber very sedative. the odious line of glass 
jar with their relics of disease and suffering stretch in front of my 
very eyes. there was no blind to the window, and a three-quarter moon 
stream its white light into the room, tracing a silver square with 
filigree lattice upon the opposite wall. when i had extinguish my 
candle this one bright patch in the midst of the general gloom had 
certainly an eerie and discompose aspect. a rigid and absolute silence 
reign throughout the old house, so that the low swish of the branch 
in the garden came soft and soothing to my ears. it may have been 
the hypnotic lullaby of this gentle susurrus, or it may have been the 
result of my tire day, but after many doze and many effort to 
regain my clear of perception, i fell at last into a deep and 
dreamless sleep. 

i was awaken by some sound in the room, and i instantly raise myself 
upon my elbow on the couch. some hour had passed, for the square patch 
upon the wall had slid downwards and sideways until it lay oblique at 
the end of my bed. the rest of the room was in deep shadow. at first i 
could see nothing, presently, as my eyes became accustomed to the faint 
light, i was aware, with a thrill which all my scientific absorption 
could not entire prevent, that something was moving slow along the 
line of the wall. a gentle, shuffle sound, as of soft slippers, came 
to my ears, and i dim discern a human figure walking stealthy from 
the direction of the door. as it emerge into the patch of moonlight i 
saw very clearly what it was and how it was employed. it was a man, 
short and squat, dress in some sort of dark-grey gown, which hung 
straight from his shoulder to his feet. the moon shone upon the side of 
his face, and i saw that it was chocolate-brown in colour, with a ball 
of black hair like a woman’s at the back of his head. he walk slowly, 
and his eyes were cast upwards towards the line of bottle which 
contained those gruesome remnant of humanity. he seem to examine each 
jar with attention, and then to pass on to the next. when he had come to 
the end of the line, immediately opposite my bed, he stopped, face me, 
threw up his hand with a gesture of despair, and vanish from my 
sight. 

i have said that he threw up his hands, but i should have said his arms, 
for as he assume that attitude of despair i observe a singular 
peculiarity about his appearance. he had only one hand! as the sleeve 
droop down from the upflung arms i saw the left plainly, but the right 
end in a knobby and unsightly stump. in every other way his appearance 
was so natural, and i had both seen and heard him so clearly, that i 
could easily have believe that he was an indian servant of sir 
dominick’s who had come into my room in search of something. it was only 
his sudden disappearance which suggest anything more sinister to me. 
as it was i sprang from my couch, lit a candle, and examine the whole 
room carefully. there were no sign of my visitor, and i was forced to 
conclude that there had really been something outside the normal law of 
nature in his appearance. i lay awake for the remainder of the night, 
but nothing else occur to disturb me. 

i am an early riser, but my uncle was an even early one, for i found 
him pace up and down the lawn at the side of the house. he ran towards 
me in his eager when he saw me come out from the door. 

“well, well!” he cried. “did you see him?” 

“an indian with one hand?” 

“precisely.” 

“yes, i saw him”—and i told him all that occurred. when i had finished, 
he led the way into his study. 

“we have a little time before breakfast,” said he. “it will suffice to 
give you an explanation of this extraordinary affair—so far as i can 
explain that which is essentially inexplicable. in the first place, when 
i tell you that for four years i have never pass one single night, 
either in bombay, aboard ship, or here in england without my sleep being 
broken by this fellow, you will understand why it is that i am a wreck 
of my former self. his programme is always the same. he appear by my 
bedside, shakes me roughly by the shoulder, pass from my room into the 
laboratory, walk slow along the line of my bottles, and then 
vanishes. for more than a thousand times he has gone through the same 
routine.” 

“what do he want?” 

“he wants his hand.” 

“his hand?” 

“yes, it came about in this way. i was summon to peshawur for a 
consultation some ten years ago, and while there i was ask to look at 
the hand of a native who was passing through with an afghanistan caravan. the 
fellow came from some mountain tribe living away at the back of beyond 
somewhere on the other side of kaffiristan. he talk a bastard pushtoo, 
and it was all i could do to understand him. he was suffering from a 
soft sarcomatous swelling of one of the metacarpal joints, and i made 
him realize that it was only by lose his hand that he could hope to 
save his life. after much persuasion he consent to the operation, and 
he ask me, when it was over, what fee i demanded. the poor fellow was 
almost a beggar, so that the idea of a fee was absurd, but i answer in 
jest that my fee should be his hand, and that i propose to add it to my 
pathological collection. 

“to my surprise he demur very much to the suggestion, and he 
explain that according to his religion it was an all-important matter 
that the body should be reunite after death, and so make a perfect 
dwelling for the spirit. the belief is, of course, an old one, and the 
mummy of the egypt arose from an analogous superstition. i 
answer him that his hand was already off, and ask him how he 
intended to preserve it. he reply that he would pickle it in salt and 
carry it about with him. i suggest that it might be safe in my 
keeping than in his, and that i had better means than salt for 
preserve it. on realize that i really intended to careful keep it, 
his opposition vanish instantly. ‘but remember, sahib,’ said he, ‘i 
shall want it back when i am dead.’ i laugh at the remark, and so the 
matter ended. i return to my practice, and he no doubt in the course 
of time was able to continue his journey to afghanistan. 

“well, as i told you last night, i had a bad fire in my house at bombay. 
half of it was burn down, and, among other things, my pathological 
collection was largely destroyed. what you see are the poor remains of 
it. the hand of the hillman went with the rest, but i gave the matter no 
particular thought at the time. that was six years ago. 

“four years ago—two years after the fire—i was awaken one night by a 
furious tug at my sleeve. i sat up under the impression that my 
favourite mastiff was try to arouse me. instead of this, i saw my 
indian patient of long ago, dress in the long grey gown which was the 
badge of his people. he was holding up his stump and look 
reproachful at me. he then went over to my bottles, which at that time 
i kept in my room, and he examine them carefully, after which he gave a 
gesture of anger and vanished. i realize that he had just died, and 
that he had come to claim my promise that i should keep his limb in 
safety for him. 

“well, there you have it all, dr. hardacre. every night at the same hour 
for four years this performance has been repeated. it is a simple thing 
in itself, but it has worn me out like water drop on a stone. it has 
brought a vile insomnia with it, for i cannot sleep now for the 
expectation of his coming. it has poison my old age and that of my 
wife, who has been the share in this great trouble. but there is the 
breakfast gong, and she will be wait impatient to know how it fare 
with you last night. we are both much indebted to you for your 
gallantry, for it take something from the weight of our misfortune when 
we share it, even for a single night, with a friend, and it reassure us 
as to our sanity, which we are sometimes driven to question.” 

this was the curious narrative which sir dominick confide to me—a story 
which to many would have appear to be a grotesque impossibility, but 
which, after my experience of the night before, and my previous 
knowledge of such things, i was prepared to accept as an absolute fact. 
i thought deep over the matter, and brought the whole range of my 
reading and experience to bear upon it. after breakfast, i surprise my 
host and hostess by announce that i was return to london by the 
next train. 

“my dear doctor,” cry sir dominick in great distress, “you make me 
feel that i have been guilty of a gross breach of hospitality in 
intrude this unfortunate matter upon you. i should have borne my own 
burden.” 

“it is, indeed, that matter which is taking me to london,” i answered; 
“but you are mistaken, i assure you, if you think that my experience of 
last night was an unpleasant one to me. on the contrary, i am about to 
ask your permission to return in the evening and spend one more night in 
your laboratory. i am very eager to see this visitor once again.” 

my uncle was exceedingly anxious to know what i was about to do, but my 
fear of raise false hope prevent me from telling him. i was back 
in my own consulting-room a little after luncheon, and was confirm my 
memory of a passage in a recent book upon occult which had arrest 
my attention when i read it. 

“in the case of earth-bound spirits,” said my authority, “some one 
dominant idea obsess them at the hour of death is sufficient to hold 
them to this material world. they are the amphibia of this life and of 
the next, capable of passing from one to the other as the turtle pass 
from land to water. the cause which may bind a soul so strong to a 
life which its body has abandoned are any violent emotion. avarice, 
revenge, anxiety, love, and pity have all been known to have this 
effect. as a rule it spring from some unfulfill wish, and when the 
wish has been fulfill the material bond relaxes. there are many case 
upon record which show the singular persistence of these visitors, and 
also their disappearance when their wish have been fulfilled, or in 
some case when a reasonable compromise has been effected.” 

“_a reasonable compromise effected_”—those were the words which i had 
brood over all the morning, and which i now verify in the original. 
no actual atone could be made here—but a reasonable compromise! i 
made my way as fast as a train could take me to the shadwell seamen’s 
hospital, where my old friend jack hewett was house-surgeon. without 
explain the situation i made him understand exactly what it was that 
i wanted. 

“a brown man’s hand!” said he, in amazement. “what in the world do you 
want that for?” 

“never mind. i’ll tell you some day. i know that your ward are full of 
indians.” 

“i should think so. but a hand——” he thought a little and then struck a 
bell. 

“travers,” said he to a student-dresser, “what became of the hand of 
the lascar which we took off yesterday? i mean the fellow from the east 
india dock who got caught in the steam winch.” 

“they are in the _post-mortem_ room, sir.” 

“just pack one of them in antiseptic and give it to dr. hardacre.” 

and so i found myself back at rodenhurst before dinner with this curious 
outcome of my day in town. i still said nothing to sir dominick, but i 
slept that night in the laboratory, and i placed the lascar’s hand in 
one of the glass jar at the end of my couch. 

so interested was i in the result of my experiment that sleep was out of 
the question. i sat with a shade lamp beside me and wait patient 
for my visitor. this time i saw him clearly from the first. he appear 
beside the door, nebulous for an instant, and then harden into as 
distinct an outline as any living man. the slipper beneath his grey 
gown were red and heelless, which account for the low, shuffle sound 
which he made as he walked. as on the previous night he pass slow 
along the line of bottle until he pause before that which contained 
the hand. he reach up to it, his whole figure quiver with 
expectation, took it down, examine it eagerly, and then, with a face 
which was convulse with fury and disappointment, he hurl it down on 
the floor. there was a crash which resound through the house, and when 
i look up the mutilate indian had disappeared. a moment later my door 
flew open and sir dominick rush in. 

“you are not hurt?” he cried. 

“no—but deep disappointed.” 

he look in astonishment at the splinter of glass, and the brown hand 
lie upon the floor. 

“good god!” he cried. “what is this?” 

i told him my idea and its wretched sequel. he listen intently, but 
shook his head. 

“it was well thought of,” said he, “but i fear that there is no such 
easy end to my sufferings. but one thing i now insist upon. it is that 
you shall never again upon any pretext occupy this room. my fear that 
something might have happen to you—when i heard that crash—have been 
the most acute of all the agony which i have undergone. i will not 
expose myself to a repetition of it.” 

he allow me, however, to spend the remainder of that night where i 
was, and i lay there worry over the problem and lament my own 
failure. with the first light of morning there was the lascar’s hand 
still lie upon the floor to remind me of my fiasco. i lay look at 
it—and as i lay sudden an idea flew like a bullet through my head and 
brought me quiver with excitement out of my couch. i raise the grim 
relic from where it had fallen. yes, it was indeed so. the hand was the 
_left_ hand of the lascar. 

by the first train i was on my way to town, and hurried at once to the 
seamen’s hospital. i remember that both hand of the lascar had been 
amputated, but i was terrify lest the precious organ which i was in 
search of might have been already consume in the crematory. my suspense 
was soon ended. it had still been preserve in the _post-mortem_ room. 
and so i return to rodenhurst in the evening with my mission 
accomplished and the material for a fresh experiment. 

but sir dominick holden would not hear of my occupy the laboratory 
again. to all my entreaty he turn a deaf ear. it offend his sense 
of hospitality, and he could no long permit it. i left the hand, 
therefore, as i had done its fellow the night before, and i occupy a 
comfortable bedroom in another portion of the house, some distance from 
the scene of my adventures. 

but in spite of that my sleep was not destined to be uninterrupted. in 
the dead of night my host burst into my room, a lamp in his hand. his 
huge gaunt figure was envelop in a loose dressing-gown, and his whole 
appearance might certainly have seem more formidable to a weak-nerved 
man than that of the indian of the night before. but it was not his 
entrance so much as his expression which amaze me. he had turn 
sudden younger by twenty years at the least. his eyes were shining, 
his features radiant, and he wave one hand in triumph over his head. i 
sat up astounded, staring sleepy at this extraordinary visitor. but 
his words soon drove the sleep from my eyes. 

“we have done it! we have succeeded!” he shouted. “my dear hardacre, how 
can i ever in this world repay you?” 

“you don’t mean to say that it is all right?” 

“indeed i do. i was sure that you would not mind being awaken to hear 
such blessed news.” 

“mind! i should think not indeed. but is it really certain?” 

“i have no doubt whatever upon the point. i owe you such a debt, my dear 
nephew, as i have never owe a man before, and never expect to. what 
can i possibly do for you that is commensurate? providence must have 
sent you to my rescue. you have save both my reason and my life, for 
another six month of this must have seen me either in a cell or a 
coffin. and my wife—it was wearing her out before my eyes. never could i 
have believe that any human being could have lift this burden off 
me.” he seize my hand and wrung it in his bony grip. 

“it was only an experiment—a forlorn hope—but i am delight from my 
heart that it has succeeded. but how do you know that it is all right? 
have you seen something?” 

he seat himself at the foot of my bed. 

“i have seen enough,” said he. “it satisfy me that i shall be trouble 
no more. what has pass is easily told. you know that at a certain hour 
this creature always come to me. to-night he arrive at the usual time, 
and arouse me with even more violence than is his custom. i can only 
surmise that his disappointment of last night increase the bitter 
of his anger against me. he look angry at me, and then went on his 
usual round. but in a few minutes i saw him, for the first time since 
this persecution began, return to my chamber. he was smiling. i saw the 
gleam of his white teeth through the dim light. he stood facing me at 
the end of my bed, and three times he made the low eastern salaam which 
is their solemn leave-taking. and the third time that he bowed he raise 
his arms over his head, and i saw his _two_ hand outstretched in the 
air. so he vanished, and, as i believe, for ever.” 

* * * * * 

so that is the curious experience which won me the affection and the 
gratitude of my celebrated uncle, the famous indian surgeon. his 
anticipation were realized, and never again was he disturbed by the 
visit of the restless hillman in search of his lost member. sir 
dominick and lady holden spent a very happy old age, unclouded, so far 
as i know, by any trouble, and they finally die during the great 
influenza epidemic within a few week of each other. in his lifetime he 
always turn to me for advice in everything which concerned that 
english life of which he knew so little; and i aid him also in the 
purchase and development of his estates. it was no great surprise to me, 
therefore, that i found myself eventually promote over the head of 
five exasperate cousins, and change in a single day from a 
hard-working country doctor into the head of an important wiltshire 
family. i at least have reason to bless the memory of the man with the 
brown hand, and the day when i was fortunate enough to relieve 
rodenhurst of his unwelcome presence. 




the fiend of the cooperage 


it was no easy matter to bring the _gamecock_ up to the island, for the 
river had swept down so much silt that the bank extend for many mile 
out into the atlantic. the coast was hardly to be seen when the first 
white curl of the breaker warn us of our danger, and from there 
onwards we made our way very careful under mainsail and jib, keeping 
the broken water well to the left, as is indicate on the chart. more 
than once her bottom touched the sand (we were drawing something under 
six feet at the time), but we had always way enough and luck enough to 
carry us through. finally, the water shoal very rapidly, but they had 
sent a canoe from the factory, and the krooboy pilot brought us within 
two hundred yard of the island. here we drop our anchor, for the 
gesture of the negro indicate that we could not hope to get any 
farther. the blue of the sea had change to the brown of the river, and, 
even under the shelter of the island, the current was singing and 
swirl round our bows. the stream appear to be in spate, for it was 
over the roots of the palm trees, and everywhere upon its muddy, greasy 
surface we could see log of wood and debris of all sort which had been 
carry down by the flood. 

when i had assured myself that we swung secure at our moorings, i 
thought it best to begin water at once, for the place look as if it 
reek with fever. the heavy river, the muddy, shining banks, the bright 
poisonous green of the jungle, the moist steam in the air, they were all 
so many danger signal to one who could read them. i sent the long-boat 
off, therefore, with two large hogsheads, which should be sufficient to 
last us until we made st. paul de loanda. for my own part i took the 
dinghy and row for the island, for i could see the union jack 
flutter above the palm to mark the position of armitage and wilson’s 
trade station. 

when i had clear the grove, i could see the place, a long, low, 
whitewash building, with a deep verandah in front, and an immense pile 
of palm oil barrel heap upon either flank of it. a row of surf boat 
and canoe lay along the beach, and a single small jetty project into 
the river. two men in white suit with red cummerbund round their 
waist were wait upon the end of it to receive me. one was a large 
portly fellow with a greyish beard. the other was slender and tall, with 
a pale pinched face, which was half conceal by a great mushroom-shaped 
hat. 

“very glad to see you,” said the latter, cordially. “i am walker, the 
agent of armitage and wilson. let me introduce dr. severall of the same 
company. it is not often we see a private yacht in these parts.” 

“she’s the _gamecock_,” i explained. “i’m owner and captain—meldrum is 
the name.” 

“exploring?” he asked. 

“i’m a lepidopterist—a butterfly-catcher. i’ve been do the west coast 
from senegal downwards.” 

“good sport?” ask the doctor, turning a slow yellow-shot eye upon me. 

“i have forty case full. we came in here to water, and also to see what 
you have in my line.” 

these introduction and explanation had fill up the time whilst my 
two krooboy were making the dinghy fast. then i walk down the jetty 
with one of my new acquaintance upon either side, each ply me with 
questions, for they had seen no white man for months. 

“what do we do?” said the doctor, when i had begun ask question in 
my turn. “our business keeps us pretty busy, and in our leisure time we 
talk politics.” 

“yes, by the special mercy of providence severall is a rank radical and 
i am a good stiff unionist, and we talk home rule for two solid hour 
every evening.” 

“and drink quinine cocktails,” said the doctor. “we’re both pretty well 
salt now, but our normal temperature was about 103 last year. i 
shouldn’t, as an impartial adviser, recommend you to stay here very long 
unless you are collect bacilli as well as butterflies. the mouth of 
the ogowai river will never develop into a health resort.” 

there is nothing fine than the way in which these outlying picket of 
civilization distil a grim humour out of their desolate situation, and 
turn not only a bold, but a laugh face upon the chance which their 
lives may bring. everywhere from sierra leone downwards i had found the 
same reek swamps, the same isolated fever-racked community and the 
same bad jokes. there is something approach to the divine in that 
power of man to rise above his conditions and to use his mind for the 
purpose of mock at the misery of his body. 

“dinner will be ready in about half an hour, captain meldrum,” said the 
doctor. “walker has gone in to see about it; he’s the housekeeper this 
week. meanwhile, if you like, we’ll stroll round and i’ll show you the 
sight of the island.” 

the sun had already sunk beneath the line of palm trees, and the great 
arch of the heaven above our head was like the inside of a huge shell, 
shimmer with dainty pink and delicate iridescence. no one who has 
not live in a land where the weight and heat of a napkin become 
intolerable upon the knee can imagine the blessed relief which the 
cool of evening bring along with it. in this sweet and pure air 
the doctor and i walk round the little island, he point out the 
stores, and explain the routine of his work. 

“there’s a certain romance about the place,” said he, in answer to some 
remark of mine about the dul of their lives. “we are living here 
just upon the edge of the great unknown. up there,” he continued, 
point to the north-east, “du chaillu penetrated, and found the home 
of the gorilla. that is the gaboon country—the land of the great apes. 
in this direction,” point to the south-east, “no one has been very 
far. the land which is drain by this river is practically unknown to 
europeans. every log which is carry past us by the current has come 
from an undiscovered country. i’ve often wish that i was a better 
botanist when i have seen the singular orchid and curious-looking 
plant which have been cast up on the eastern end of the island.” 

the place which the doctor indicate was a slope brown beach, freely 
litter with the flotsam of the stream. at each end was a curve point, 
like a little natural breakwater, so that a small shallow bay was left 
between. this was full of floating vegetation, with a single huge 
splinter tree lie stranded in the middle of it, the current ripple 
against its high black side. 

“these are all from up country,” said the doctor. “they get caught in 
our little bay, and then when some extra freshet come they are wash 
out again and carry out to sea.” 

“what is the tree?” i asked. 

“oh, some kind of teak i should imagine, but pretty rotten by the look 
of it. we get all sort of big hardwood tree floating past here, to say 
nothing of the palms. just come in here, will you?” 

he led the way into a long building with an immense quantity of barrel 
staves and iron hoop litter about in it. 

“this is our cooperage,” said he. “we have the staves sent out in 
bundles, and we put them together ourselves. now, you don’t see anything 
particularly sinister about this building, do you?” 

i look round at the high corrugate iron roof, the white wooden walls, 
and the earthen floor. in one corner lay a mattress and a blanket. 

“i see nothing very alarming,” said i. 

“and yet there’s something out of the common, too,” he remarked. “you 
see that bed? well, i intend to sleep there to-night. i don’t want to 
buck, but i think it’s a bit of a test for nerve.” 

“why?” 

“oh, there have been some funny goings on. you were talk about the 
monotony of our lives, but i assure you that they are sometimes quite as 
exciting as we wish them to be. you’d better come back to the house now, 
for after sundown we begin to get the fever-fog up from the marshes. 
there, you can see it coming across the river.” 

i look and saw long tentacle of white vapour writhe out from among 
the thick green underwood and crawl at us over the broad swirl 
surface of the brown river. at the same time the air turn sudden 
dank and cold. 

“there’s the dinner gong,” said the doctor. “if this matter interests 
you i’ll tell you about it afterwards.” 

it did interest me very much, for there was something earnest and 
subdued in his manner as he stood in the empty cooperage, which appeal 
very forcibly to my imagination. he was a big, bluff, hearty man, this 
doctor, and yet i had detect a curious expression in his eyes as he 
glance about him—an expression which i would not describe as one of 
fear, but rather that of a man who is alert and on his guard. 

“by the way,” said i, as we return to the house, “you have shown me 
the hut of a good many of your native assistants, but i have not seen 
any of the native themselves.” 

“they sleep in the hulk over yonder,” the doctor answered, point over 
to one of the banks. 

“indeed. i should not have thought in that case that they would need the 
huts.” 

“oh, they use the hut until quite recently. we’ve put them on the hulk 
until they recover their confidence a little. they were all half mad 
with fright, so we let them go, and nobody sleep on the island except 
walker and myself.” 

“what frightened them?” i asked. 

“well, that bring us back to the same story. i suppose walker has no 
objection to your hearing all about it. i don’t know why we should make 
any secret about it, though it is certainly a pretty bad business.” 

he made no further allusion to it during the excellent dinner which had 
been prepared in my honour. it appear that no soon had the little 
white topsail of the _gamecock_ shown round cape lopez than these kind 
fellow had begun to prepare their famous pepper-pot—which is the
