pungent stew peculiar to the west coast—and to boil their yam and sweet 
potatoes. we sat down to as good a native dinner as one could wish, 
serve by a smart sierra leone wait boy. i was just remark to 
myself that he at least had not share in the general flight when, 
have laid the dessert and wine upon the table, he raise his hand to 
his turban. 

“anyting else i do, massa walker?” he asked. 

“no, i think that is all right, moussa,” my host answered. “i am not 
feeling very well to-night, though, and i should much prefer if you 
would stay on the island.” 

i saw a struggle between his fear and his duty upon the swarthy face of 
the african. his skin had turn of that livid purplish tint which 
stand for pallor in a negro, and his eyes look furtive about him. 

“no, no, massa walker,” he cried, at last, “you better come to the hulk 
with me, sah. look after you much better in the hulk, sah!” 

“that won’t do, moussa. white men don’t run away from the post where 
they are placed.” 

again i saw the passionate struggle in the negro’s face, and again his 
fear prevailed. 

“no use, massa walker, sah!” he cried. “s’elp me, i can’t do it. if it 
was yesterday or if it was to-morrow, but this is the third night, sah, 
an’ it’s more than i can face.” 

walker shrug his shoulders. 

“off with you then!” said he. “when the mail-boat come you can get back 
to sierra leone, for i’ll have no servant who deserts me when i need him 
most. i suppose this is all mystery to you, or has the doctor told you, 
captain meldrum?” 

“i show captain meldrum the cooperage, but i did not tell him 
anything,” said dr. severall. “you’re look bad, walker,” he added, 
glancing at his companion. “you have a strong touch coming on you.” 

“yes, i’ve had the shivers all day, and now my head is like a 
cannon-ball. i took ten grain of quinine, and my ear are singing like 
a kettle. but i want to sleep with you in the cooperage to-night.” 

“no, no, my dear chap. i won’t hear of such a thing. you must get to bed 
at once, and i am sure meldrum will excuse you. i shall sleep in the 
cooperage, and i promise you that i’ll be round with your medicine 
before breakfast.” 

it was evident that walker had been struck by one of those sudden and 
violent attack of remittent fever which are the curse of the west 
coast. his sallow cheek were flushed and his eyes shining with fever, 
and sudden as he sat there he began to croon out a song in the 
high-pitched voice of delirium. 

“come, come, we must get you to bed, old chap,” said the doctor, and 
with my aid he led his friend into his bedroom. there we undressed him, 
and presently, after taking a strong sedative, he settled down into a 
deep slumber. 

“he’s right for the night,” said the doctor, as we sat down and fill 
our glasses once more. “sometimes it is my turn and sometimes his, but, 
fortunately, we have never been down together. i should have been sorry 
to be out of it to-night, for i have a little mystery to unravel. i told 
you that i intended to sleep in the cooperage.” 

“yes, you said so.” 

“when i said sleep i meant watch, for there will be no sleep for me. 
we’ve had such a scare here that no native will stay after sundown, and 
i mean to find out to-night what the cause of it all may be. it has 
always been the custom for a native watchman to sleep in the cooperage, 
to prevent the barrel hoop being stolen. well, six days ago the fellow 
who slept there disappeared, and we have never seen a trace of him 
since. it was certainly singular, for no canoe had been taken, and these 
waters are too full of crocodile for any man to swim to shore. what 
became of the fellow, or how he could have left the island is a complete 
mystery. walker and i were merely surprised, but the black were badly 
scared, and queer voodoo tale began to get about amongst them. but the 
real stampede broke out three nights ago, when the new watchman in the 
cooperage also disappeared.” 

“what became of him?” i asked. 

“well, we not only don’t know, but we can’t even give a guess which 
would fit the facts. the nigger swear there is a fiend in the cooperage 
who claim a man every third night. they wouldn’t stay in the 
island—nothing could persuade them. even moussa, who is a faithful boy 
enough, would, as you have seen, leave his master in a fever rather than 
remain for the night. if we are to continue to run this place we must 
reassure our niggers, and i don’t know any better way of do it than 
by putt in a night there myself. this is the third night, you see, so 
i suppose the thing is due, whatever it may be.” 

“have you no clue?” i asked. “was there no mark of violence, no 
blood-stain, no footprints, nothing to give a hint as to what kind of 
danger you may have to meet?” 

“absolutely nothing. the man was gone and that was all. last time it was 
old ali, who has been wharf-tender here since the place was started. he 
was always as steady as a rock, and nothing but foul play would take him 
from his work.” 

“well,” said i, “i really don’t think that this is a one-man job. your 
friend is full of laudanum, and come what might he can be of no 
assistance to you. you must let me stay and put in a night with you at 
the cooperage.” 

“well, now, that’s very good of you, meldrum,” said he heartily, shake 
my hand across the table. “it’s not a thing that i should have venture 
to propose, for it is ask a good deal of a casual visitor, but if you 
really mean it——” 

“certainly i mean it. if you will excuse me a moment, i will hail the 
_gamecock_ and let them know that they need not expect me.” 

as we came back from the other end of the little jetty we were both 
struck by the appearance of the night. a huge blue-black pile of cloud 
had built itself up upon the landward side, and the wind came from it in 
little hot pants, which beat upon our face like the draught from a 
blast furnace. under the jetty the river was swirl and hissing, 
toss little white spurt of spray over the planking. 

“confound it!” said doctor severall. “we are likely to have a flood on 
the top of all our troubles. that rise in the river means heavy rain 
up-country, and when it once begin you never know how far it will go. 
we’ve had the island nearly cover before now. well, we’ll just go and 
see that walker is comfortable, and then if you like we’ll settle down 
in our quarters.” 

the sick man was sunk in a profound slumber, and we left him with some 
crush lime in a glass beside him in case he should awake with the 
thirst of fever upon him. then we made our way through the unnatural 
gloom thrown by that menace cloud. the river had risen so high that 
the little bay which i have describe at the end of the island had 
become almost obliterate through the submerge of its flank 
peninsula. the great raft of driftwood, with the huge black tree in the 
middle, was sway up and down in the swollen current. 

“that’s one good thing a flood will do for us,” said the doctor. “it 
carry away all the vegetable stuff which is brought down on to the 
east end of the island. it came down with the freshet the other day, and 
here it will stay until a flood sweep it out into the main stream. 
well, here’s our room, and here are some books, and here is my tobacco 
pouch, and we must try and put in the night as best we may.” 

by the light of our single lantern the great lonely room look very 
gaunt and dreary. save for the piles of staves and heap of hoop there 
was absolutely nothing in it, with the exception of the mattress for the 
doctor, which had been laid in the corner. we made a couple of seat and 
a table out of the staves, and settled down together for a long vigil. 
severall had brought a revolver for me, and was himself armed with a 
double-barrelled shot-gun. we loaded our weapon and laid them cock 
within reach of our hands. the little circle of light and the black 
shadow arch over us were so melancholy that he went off to the 
house, and return with two candles. one side of the cooperage was 
pierced, however, by several open windows, and it was only by screening 
our lights behind staves that we could prevent them from being 
extinguished. 

the doctor, who appear to be a man of iron nerves, had settled down to 
a book, but i observe that every now and then he laid it upon his knee, 
and took an earnest look all round him. for my part, although i try 
once or twice to read, i found it impossible to concentrate my thought 
upon the book. they would always wander back to this great empty silent 
room, and to the sinister mystery which overshadow it. i rack my 
brains for some possible theory which would explain the disappearance of 
these two men. there was the black fact that they were gone, and not the 
least tittle of evidence as to why or whither. and here we were wait 
in the same place—waiting without an idea as to what we were wait 
for. i was right in saying that it was not a one-man job. it was try 
enough as it was, but no force upon earth would have kept me there 
without a comrade. 

what an endless, tedious night it was! outside we heard the lap and 
gurgle of the great river, and the sough of the rising wind. 
within, save for our breathing, the turning of the doctor’s pages, and 
the high, shrill ping of an occasional mosquito, there was a heavy 
silence. once my heart sprang into my mouth as severall’s book sudden 
fell to the ground and he sprang to his feet with his eyes on one of the 
windows. 

“did you see anything, meldrum?” 

“no. did you?” 

“well, i had a vague sense of movement outside that window.” he caught 
up his gun and approach it. “no, there’s nothing to be seen, and yet i 
could have sworn that something pass slow across it.” 

“a palm leaf, perhaps,” said i, for the wind was grow strong every 
instant. 

“very likely,” said he, and settled down to his book again, but his eyes 
were for ever dart little suspicious glance up at the window. i 
watch it also, but all was quiet outside. 

and then sudden our thought were turn into a new direction by the 
burst of the storm. a blind flash was follow by a clap which 
shook the building. again and again came the vivid white glare with 
thunder at the same instant, like the flash and roar of a monstrous 
piece of artillery. and then down came the tropical rain, crashing and 
rattling on the corrugate iron roofing of the cooperage. the big hollow 
room boom like a drum. from the dark arose a strange mixture of 
noises, a gurgling, splashing, tinkling, bubbling, washing, 
dripping—every liquid sound that nature can produce from the thrashing 
and swish of the rain to the deep steady boom of the river. hour 
after hour the uproar grew loud and more sustained. 

“my word,” said severall, “we are go to have the father of all the 
flood this time. well, here’s the dawn coming at last and that is a 
blessing. we’ve about exploded the third night superstition anyhow.” 

a grey light was steal through the room, and there was the day upon 
us in an instant. the rain had ease off, but the coffee-coloured river 
was roaring past like a waterfall. its power made me fear for the anchor 
of the _gamecock_. 

“i must get aboard,” said i. “if she drag she’ll never be able to beat 
up the river again.” 

“the island is as good as a breakwater,” the doctor answered. “i can 
give you a cup of coffee if you will come up to the house.” 

i was chill and miserable, so the suggestion was a welcome one. we 
left the ill-omened cooperage with its mystery still unsolved, and we 
splash our way up to the house. 

“there’s the spirit lamp,” said severall. “if you would just put a light 
to it, i will see how walker feel this morning.” 

he left me, but was back in an instant with a dreadful face. 

“he’s gone!” he cry hoarsely. 

the words sent a thrill of horror through me. i stood with the lamp in 
my hand, glaring at him. 

“yes, he’s gone!” he repeated. “come and look!” 

i follow him without a word, and the first thing that i saw as i 
enter the bedroom was walker himself lie huddle on his bed in the 
grey flannel sleep suit in which i had help to dress him on the 
night before. 

“not dead, surely!” i gasped. 

the doctor was terribly agitated. his hand were shake like leaves in 
the wind. 

“he’s been dead some hours.” 

“was it fever?” 

“fever! look at his foot!” 

i glance down and a cry of horror burst from my lips. one foot was not 
merely dislocate but was turn completely round in a most grotesque 
contortion. 

“good god!” i cried. “what can have done this?” 

severall had laid his hand upon the dead man’s chest. 

“feel here,” he whispered. 

i placed my hand at the same spot. there was no resistance. the body was 
absolutely soft and limp. it was like pressing a sawdust doll. 

“the breast-bone is gone,” said severall in the same awed whisper. “he’s 
broken to bits. thank god that he had the laudanum. you can see by his 
face that he die in his sleep.” 

“but who can have done this?” 

“i’ve had about as much as i can stand,” said the doctor, wipe his 
forehead. “i don’t know that i’m a greater coward than my neighbours, 
but this get beyond me. if you’re go out to the _gamecock_——” 

“come on!” said i, and off we started. if we did not run it was because 
each of us wish to keep up the last shadow of his self-respect before 
the other. it was dangerous in a light canoe on that swollen river, but 
we never pause to give the matter a thought. he bail and i paddle 
we kept her above water, and gain the deck of the yacht. there, with 
two hundred yard of water between us and this cursed island, we felt 
that we were our own men once more. 

“well go back in an hour or so,” said he. “but we need a little time to 
steady ourselves. i wouldn’t have had the nigger see me as i was just 
now for a year’s salary.” 

“i’ve told the steward to prepare breakfast. then we shall go back,” 
said i. “but in god’s name, doctor severall, what do you make of it 
all?” 

“it beat me—beats me clean. i’ve heard of voodoo devilry, and i’ve 
laugh at it with the others. but that poor old walker, a decent, 
god-fearing, nineteenth-century, primrose-league englishman should go 
under like this without a whole bone in his body—it’s given me a shake, 
i won’t deny it. but look there, meldrum, is that hand of yours mad or 
drunk, or what is it?” 

old patterson, the oldest man of my crew, and as steady as the pyramids, 
had been station in the bow with a boat-hook to fend off the drift 
log which came sweeping down with the current. now he stood with 
crooked knees, glaring out in front of him, and one forefinger stabbing 
furiously at the air. 

“look at it!” he yelled. “look at it!” 

and at the same instant we saw it. 

a huge black tree trunk was coming down the river, its broad glisten 
back just lap by the water. and in front of it—about three feet in 
front—arching upwards like the figure-head of a ship, there hung a 
dreadful face, sway slow from side to side. it was flattened, 
malignant, as large as a small beer-barrel, of a fade fungoid colour, 
but the neck which support it was mottled with a dull yellow and 
black. as it flew past the _gamecock_ in the swirl of the waters i saw 
two immense coil roll up out of some great hollow in the tree, and the 
villainous head rose sudden to the height of eight or ten feet, 
look with dull, skin-covered eyes at the yacht. an instant later the 
tree had shot past us and was plunge with its horrible passenger 
towards the atlantic. 

“what was it?” i cried. 

“it is our fiend of the cooperage,” said dr. severall, and he had become 
in an instant the same bluff, self-confident man that he had been 
before. “yes, that is the devil who has been haunting our island. it is 
the great python of the gaboon.” 

i thought of the story which i had heard all down the coast of the 
monstrous constrictor of the interior, of their periodical appetite, 
and of the murderous effects of their deadly squeeze. then it all took 
shape in my mind. there had been a freshet the week before. it had 
brought down this huge hollow tree with its hideous occupant. who know 
from what far distant tropical forest it may have come. it had been 
stranded on the little east bay of the island. the cooperage had been 
the nearest house. twice with the return of its appetite it had carry 
off the watchman. last night it had doubtless come again, when severall 
had thought he saw something move at the window, but our lights had 
driven it away. it had writhe onwards and had slain poor walker in his 
sleep. 

“why did it not carry him off?” i asked. 

“the thunder and lightning must have scared the brute away. there’s your 
steward, meldrum. the soon we have breakfast and get back to the 
island the better, or some of those nigger might think that we had been 
frightened.” 




jelland’s voyage 


“well,” said our anglo-jap as we all drew up our chair round the 
smoking-room fire, “it’s an old tale out yonder, and may have spilt over 
into print for all i know. i don’t want to turn this club-room into a 
chestnut stall, but it is a long way to the yellow sea, and it is just 
as likely that none of you have ever heard of the yawl _matilda_, and of 
what happen to henry jelland and wil mcevoy aboard of her. 

“the middle of the sixty was a stirring time out in japan. that was 
just after the simonosaki bombardment, and before the daimio affair. 
there was a tory party and there was a liberal party among the natives, 
and the question that they were wrangle over was whether the throat 
of the foreigner should be cut or not. i tell you all, politics have 
been tame to me since then. if you live in a treaty port, you were 
bound to wake up and take an interest in them. and to make it better, 
the outsider had no way of knowing how the game was going. if the 
opposition won it would not be a newspaper paragraph that would tell him 
of it, but a good old tory in a suit of chain mail, with a sword in each 
hand, would drop in and let him know all about it in a single upper cut. 

“of course it make men reckless when they are living on the edge of a 
volcano like that. just at first they are very jumpy, and then there 
come a time when they learn to enjoy life while they have it. i tell 
you, there’s nothing make life so beautiful as when the shadow of death 
begin to fall across it. time is too precious to be dawdle away then, 
and a man lives every minute of it. that was the way with us in 
yokohama. there were many europe place of business which had to go on 
running, and the men who work them made the place lively for seven 
nights in the week. 

“one of the head of the europe colony was randolph moore, the big 
export merchant. his offices were in yokohama, but he spent a good deal 
of his time at his house up in jeddo, which had only just been open to 
the trade. in his absence he use to leave his affair in the hand of 
his head clerk, jelland, whom he knew to be a man of great energy and 
resolution. but energy and resolution are two-edged things, you know, 
and when they are use against you you don’t appreciate them so much. 

“it was gamble that set jelland wrong. he was a little dark-eyed 
fellow with black curly hair—more than three-quarters celt, i should 
imagine. every night in the week you would see him in the same place, on 
the left-hand side of the croupier at matheson’s _rouge et noir_ table. 
for a long time he won, and live in better style than his employer. and 
then came a turn of luck, and he began to lose so that at the end of a 
single week his partner and he were stone broke, without a dollar to 
their names. 

“this partner was a clerk in the employ of the same firm—a tall, 
straw-haired young englishman call mcevoy. he was a good boy enough at 
the start, but he was clay in the hand of jelland, who fashion him 
into a kind of weak model of himself. they were for ever on the prowl 
together, but it was jelland who led and mcevoy who followed. lynch and 
i and one or two other try to show the youngster that he could come 
to no good along that line, and when we were talk to him we could win 
him round easily enough, but five minutes of jelland would swing him 
back again. it may have been animal magnetism or what you like, but the 
little man could pull the big one along like a sixty-foot tug in front 
of a full-rigged ship. even when they had lost all their money they 
would still take their place at the table and look on with shining eyes 
when any one else was rake in the stamps. 

“but one evening they could keep out of it no longer. red had turn up 
sixteen times running, and it was more than jelland could bear. he 
whisper to mcevoy, and then said a word to the croupier. 

“‘certainly, mr. jelland; your cheque is as good as notes,’ said he. 

“jelland scribble a cheque and threw it on the black. the card was the 
king of hearts, and the croupier rake in the little bit of paper. 
jelland grew angry, and mcevoy white. another and a heavy cheque was 
written and thrown on the table. the card was the nine of diamonds. 
mcevoy lean his head upon his hand and look as if he would faint. 
‘by god!’ growl jelland, ‘i won’t be beat,’ and he threw on a cheque 
that cover the other two. the card was the deuce of hearts. a few 
minutes later they were walking down the bund, with the cool night-air 
play upon their fevered faces. 

“‘of course you know what this means,’ said jelland, lighting a cheroot; 
‘we’ll have to transfer some of the office money to our current account. 
there’s no occasion to make a fuss over it. old moore won’t look over 
the books before easter. if we have any luck, we can easily replace it 
before then.’ 

“‘but if we have no luck?’ falter mcevoy. 

“‘tut, man, we must take things as they come. you stick to me, and i’ll 
stick to you, and we’ll pull through together. you shall sign the 
cheque to-morrow night, and we shall see if your luck is better than 
mine.’ 

“but if anything it was worse. when the pair rose from the table on the 
following evening, they had spent over £5,000 of their employer’s money. 
but the resolute jelland was as sanguine as ever. 

“‘we have a good nine week before us before the books will be 
examined,’ said he. ‘we must play the game out, and it will all come 
straight.’ 

“mcevoy return to his rooms that night in an agony of shame and 
remorse. when he was with jelland he borrow strength from him; but 
alone he recognize the full danger of his position, and the vision of 
his old white-capped mother in england, who had been so proud when he 
had received his appointment, rose up before him to fill him with 
loathing and madness. he was still toss upon his sleepless couch when 
his japan servant enter the bedroom. for an instant mcevoy thought 
that the long-expected outbreak had come, and plunge for his revolver. 
then, with his heart in his mouth, he listen to the message which the 
servant had brought. 

“jelland was downstairs, and want to see him. 

“what on earth could he want at that hour of night? mcevoy dress 
hurried and rush downstairs. his companion, with a set smile upon 
his lips, which was belie by the ghastly pallor of his face, was 
sitting in the dim light of a solitary candle, with a slip of paper in 
his hands. 

“‘sorry to knock you up, willy,’ said he. ‘no eavesdroppers, i suppose?’ 

“mcevoy shook his head. he could not trust himself to speak. 

“‘well, then, our little game is play out. this note was wait for 
me at home. it is from moore, and say that he will be down on monday 
morning for an examination of the books. it leaves us in a tight place.’ 

“‘monday!’ gasp mcevoy; ‘to-day is friday.’ 

“‘saturday, my son, and 3 a.m. we have not much time to turn round in.’ 

“‘we are lost!’ scream mcevoy. 

“‘we soon will be, if you make such an infernal row,’ said jelland 
harshly. ‘now do what i tell you, willy, and we’ll pull through yet.’ 

“‘i will do anything—anything.’ 

“‘that’s better. where’s your whisky? it’s a beastly time of the day to 
have to get your back stiff, but there must be no soft with us, or 
we are gone. first of all, i think there is something due to our 
relations, don’t you?’ 

“mcevoy stared. 

“‘we must stand or fall together, you know. now i, for one, don’t intend 
to set my foot inside a felon’s dock under any circumstances. d’ye see? 
i’m ready to swear to that. are you?’ 

“‘what d’you mean?’ ask mcevoy, shrink back. 

“‘why, man, we all have to die, and it’s only the pressing of a trigger. 
i swear that i shall never be taken alive. will you? if you don’t, i 
leave you to your fate.’ 

“‘all right. i’ll do whatever you think best.’ 

“‘you swear it?’ 

“‘yes.’ 

“‘well, mind, you must be as good as your word. now we have two clear 
days to get off in. the yawl _matilda_ is on sale, and she has all her 
fix and plenty of tin stuff aboard. we’ll buy the lot to-morrow 
morning, and whatever we want, and get away in her. but, first, we’ll 
clear all that is left in the office. there are 5,000 sovereign in the 
safe. after dark we’ll get them aboard the yawl, and take our chance of 
reach california. there’s no use hesitating, my son, for we have no 
ghost of a look-in in any other direction. it’s that or nothing.’ 

“‘i’ll do what you advise.’ 

“‘all right; and mind you get a bright face on you to-morrow, for if 
moore get the tip and come before monday, then——’ he tap the 
side-pocket of his coat and look across at his partner with eyes that 
were full of a sinister meaning. 

“all went well with their plan next day. the _matilda_ was bought 
without difficulty; and, though she was a tiny craft for so long a 
voyage, had she been large two men could not have hope to manage her. 
she was stock with water during the day, and after dark the two clerk 
brought down the money from the office and stow it in the hold. before 
midnight they had collected all their own possession without exciting 
suspicion, and at two in the morning they left their moorings and stole 
quiet out from among the shipping. they were seen, of course, and were 
set down as keen yachtsmen who were on for a good long sunday cruise; 
but there was no one who dream that that cruise would only end either 
on the america coast or at the bottom of the north pacific ocean. 
strain and hauling, they got their mainsail up and set their foresail 
and jib. there was a slight breeze from the south-east, and the little 
craft went dip along upon her way. seven mile from land, however, 
the wind fell away and they lay becalmed, rising and fall on the long 
swell of a glassy sea. all sunday they did not make a mile, and in the 
evening yokohama still lay along the horizon. 

“on monday morning down came randolph moore from jeddo, and made 
straight for the offices. he had had the tip from some one that his 
clerk had been spread themselves a bit, and that had made him come 
down out of his usual routine; but when he reach his place and found 
the three junior wait in the street with their hand in their 
pocket he knew that the matter was serious. 

“‘what’s this?’ he asked. he was a man of action, and a nasty chap to 
deal with when he had his topmast lowered. 

“‘we can’t get in,’ said the clerks. 

“‘where is mr. jelland?’ 

“‘he has not come to-day.’ 

“‘and mr. mcevoy?’ 

“‘he has not come either.’ 

“randolph moore look serious. ‘we must have the door down,’ said he. 

“they don’t build house very solid in that land of earthquakes, and in 
a brace of shakes they were all in the office. of course the thing told 
its own story. the safe was open, the money gone, and the clerk fled. 
their employer lost no time in talk. 

“‘where were they seen last?’ 

“‘on saturday they bought the _matilda_ and start for a cruise.’ 

“saturday! the matter seem hopeless if they had got two days’ start. 
but there was still the shadow of a chance. he rush to the beach and 
swept the ocean with his glasses. 

“‘my god!’ he cried. ‘there’s the _matilda_ out yonder. i know her by 
the rake of her mast. i have my hand upon the villain after all!’ 

“but there was a hitch even then. no boat had steam up, and the eager 
merchant had not patience to wait. cloud were banking up along the 
haunch of the hills, and there was every sign of an approach change 
of weather. a police boat was ready with ten armed men in her, and 
randolph moore himself took the tiller as she shot out in pursuit of the 
becalmed yawl. 

“jelland and mcevoy, wait weary for the breeze which never came, 
saw the dark speck which sprang out from the shadow of the land and grew 
large with every swish of the oars. as she drew nearer, they could see 
also that she was packed with men, and the gleam of weapon told what 
manner of men they were. jelland stood leaning against the tiller, and 
he look at the threaten sky, the limp sails, and the approach 
boat. 

“‘it’s a case with us, willy,’ said he. ‘by the lord, we are two most 
unlucky devils, for there’s wind in that sky, and another hour would 
have brought it to us.’ 

“mcevoy groaned. 

“‘there’s no good soften over it, my lad,’ said jelland. ‘it’s the 
police boat right enough, and there’s old moore driving them to row like 
hell. it’ll be a ten-dollar job for every man of them.’ 

“willy mcevoy crouch against the side with his knee on the deck. ‘my 
mother! my poor old mother!’ he sobbed. 

“‘she’ll never hear that you have been in the dock anyway,’ said 
jelland. ‘my people never did much for me, but i will do that much for 
them. it’s no good, mac. we can chuck our hands. god bless you, old man! 
here’s the pistol!’ 

“he cock the revolver, and held the butt towards the youngster. but 
the other shrunk away from it with little gasp and cries. jelland 
glance at the approach boat. it was not more than a few hundred 
yard away. 

“‘there’s no time for nonsense,’ said he. ‘damn it! man, what’s the use 
of flinching? you swore it!’ 

“‘no, no, jelland!’ 

“‘well, anyhow, i swore that neither of us should be taken. will you do 
it?’ 

“‘i can’t! i can’t!’ 

“‘then i will for you.’ 

“the rower in the boat saw him lean forwards, they heard two pistol 
shots, they saw him double himself across the tiller, and then, before 
the smoke had lifted, they found that they had something else to think 
of. 

“for at that instant the storm broke—one of those short sudden squall 
which are common in these seas. the _matilda_ heel over, her sail 
belly out, she plunge her lee-rail into a wave, and was off like a 
frightened deer. jelland’s body had jam the helm, and she kept a 
course right before the wind, and flutter away over the rising sea 
like a blown piece of paper. the rower work frantically, but the yawl 
still drew ahead, and in five minutes it had plunge into the storm 
wrack never to be seen again by mortal eye. the boat put back, and 
reach yokohama with the water washing half-way up to the thwarts. 

“and that was how it came that the yawl _matilda_, with a cargo of five 
thousand pound and a crew of two dead young men, set sail across the 
pacific ocean. what the end of jelland’s voyage may have been no man 
knows. he may have founder in that gale, or he may have been picked up 
by some canny merchantman, who stuck to the bullion and kept his mouth 
shut, or he may still be cruise in that vast waste of waters, blown 
north to the behr sea, or south to the malay islands. it’s better to 
leave it unfinished than to spoil a true story by invent a tag to 
it.” 




b. 24 


i told my story when i was taken, and no one would listen to me. then i 
told it again at the trial—the whole thing absolutely as it happened, 
without so much as a word added. i set it all out truly, so help me god, 
all that lady manner said and did, and then all that i had said and 
done, just as it occurred. and what did i get for it? “the prisoner put 
forward a rambling and inconsequential statement, incredible in its 
details, and unsupported by any shred of corroborative evidence.” that 
was what one of the london papers said, and other let it pass as if i 
had made no defence at all. and yet, with my own eyes i saw lord 
manner murdered, and i am as guiltless of it as any man on the jury 
that try me. 

now, sir, you are there to receive the petition of prisoners. it all 
lie with you. all i ask is that you read it—just read it—and then that 
you make an inquiry or two about the private character of this “lady” 
mannering, if she still keeps the name that she had three years ago, 
when to my sorrow and ruin i came to meet her. you could use a private 
inquiry agent or a good lawyer, and you would soon learn enough to show 
you that my story is the true one. think of the glory it would be to you 
to have all the papers saying that there would have been a shocking 
miscarry of justice if it had not been for your perseverance and 
intelligence! that must be your reward, since i am a poor man and can 
offer you nothing. but if you don’t do it, may you never lie easy in 
your bed again! may no night pass that you are not haunt by the 
thought of the man who rot in gaol because you have not done the duty 
which you are paid to do! but you will do it, sir, i know. just make one 
or two inquiries, and you will soon find which way the wind blows. 
remember, also, that the only person who profit by the crime was 
herself, since it change her from an unhappy wife to a rich young 
widow. there’s the end of the string in your hand, and you only have to 
follow it up and see where it leads to. 

mind you, sir, i make no complaint as far as the burglary goes. i don’t 
whine about what i have deserved, and so far i have had no more than i 
have deserved. burglary it was, right enough, and my three years have 
gone to pay for it. it was shown at the trial that i had had a hand in 
the merton cross business, and did a year for that, so my story had the 
less attention on that account. a man with a previous conviction never 
get a really fair trial. i own to the burglary, but when it come to 
the murder which brought me a lifer—any judge but sir james might have 
given me the gallows—then i tell you that i had nothing to do with it, 
and that i am an innocent man. and now i’ll take that night, the 13th of 
september, 1894, and i’ll give you just exactly what occurred, and may 
god’s hand strike me down if i go one inch over the truth. 

i had been at bristol in the summer look for work, and then i had a 
notion that i might get something at portsmouth, for i was train as a 
skilled mechanic, so i came tramp my way across the south of england, 
and do odd job as i went. i was try all i knew to keep off the 
cross, for i had done a year in exeter gaol, and i had had enough of 
visiting queen victoria. but it’s cruel hard to get work when once the 
black mark is against your name, and it was all i could do to keep soul 
and body together. at last, after ten days of wood-cutting and 
stone-breaking on starvation pay, i found myself near salisbury with a 
couple of shilling in my pocket, and my boots and my patience clean 
wore out. there’s an ale-house call “the willing mind,” which stand 
on the road between blandford and salisbury, and it was there that night 
i engaged a bed. i was sitting alone in the tap-room just about close 
time, when the innkeeper—allen his name was—came beside me and began 
yarn about the neighbours. he was a man that like to talk and to 
have some one to listen to his talk, so i sat there smoking and drink 
a mug of ale which he had stood me; and i took no great interest in what 
he said until he began to talk (as the devil would have it) about the 
riches of manner hall. 

“meaning the large house on the right before i came to the village?” 
said i. “the one that stand in its own park?” 

“exactly,” said he—and i am give all our talk so that you may know 
that i am telling you the truth and hiding nothing. “the long white 
house with the pillars,” said he. “at the side of the blandford road.” 

now i had look at it as i passed, and it had cross my mind, as such 
thought will, that it was a very easy house to get into with that great 
row of ground window and glass doors. i had put the thought away from 
me, and now here was this landlord bring it back with his talk about 
the riches within. i said nothing, but i listened, and as luck would 
have it, he would always come back to this one subject. 

“he was a miser young, so you can think what he is now in his age,” said 
he. “well, he’s had some good out of his money.” 

“what good can he have had if he do not spend it?” said i. 

“well, it bought him the prettiest wife in england, and that was some 
good that he got out of it. she thought she would have the spend of 
it, but she know the difference now.” 

“who was she then?” i asked, just for the sake of something to say. 

“she was nobody at all until the old lord made her his lady,” said he. 
“she came from up london way, and some said that she had been on the 
stage there, but nobody knew. the old lord was away for a year, and when 
he came home he brought a young wife back with him, and there she has 
been ever since. stephens, the butler, did tell me once that she was the 
light of the house when fust she came, but what with her husband’s mean 
and aggravatin’ way, and what with her loneliness—for he hate to see a 
visitor within his doors; and what with his bitter words—for he has a 
tongue like a hornet’s sting, her life all went out of her, and she 
became a white, silent creature, mope about the country lanes. some 
say that she love another man, and that it was just the riches of the 
old lord which tempt her to be false to her lover, and that now she is 
eat her heart out because she has lost the one without being any 
near to the other, for she might be the poorest woman in the parish 
for all the money that she has the handle of.” 

well, sir, you can imagine that it did not interest me very much to hear 
about the quarrel between a lord and a lady. what did it matter to me 
if she hate the sound of his voice, or if he put every indignity upon 
her in the hope of break her spirit, and spoke to her as he would 
never have dare to speak to one of his servants? the landlord told me 
of these things, and of many more like them, but they pass out of my 
mind, for they were no concern of mine. but what i did want to hear was 
the form in which lord manner kept his riches. title-deeds and stock 
certificate are but paper, and more danger than profit to the man who 
take them. but metal and stone are worth a risk. and then, as if he 
were answer my very thoughts, the landlord told me of lord 
mannering’s great collection of gold medals, that it was the most 
valuable in the world, and that it was reckon that if they were put 
into a sack the strongest man in the parish would not be able to raise 
them. then his wife call him, and he and i went to our beds. 

i am not argue to make out a case for myself, but i beg you, sir, to 
bear all the fact in your mind, and to ask yourself whether a man could 
be more sorely tempt than i was. i make bold to say that there are few 
who could have held out against it. there i lay on my bed that night, a 
desperate man without hope or work, and with my last shilling in my 
pocket. i had try to be honest, and honest folk had turn their back 
upon me. they taunt me for theft; and yet they pushed me towards it. i 
was caught in the stream and could not get out. and then it was such a 
chance: the great house all line with windows, the golden medal which 
could so easily be melt down. it was like putt a loaf before a 
starve man and expect him not to eat it. i fought against it for a 
time, but it was no use. at last i sat up on the side of my bed, and i 
swore that that night i should either be a rich man and able to give up 
crime for ever, or that the irons should be on my wrist once more. then 
i slip on my clothes, and, have put a shilling on the table—for the 
landlord had treat me well, and i did not wish to cheat him—i pass 
out through the window into the garden of the inn. 

there was a high wall round this garden, and i had a job to get over it, 
but once on the other side it was all plain sailing. i did not meet a 
soul upon the road, and the iron gate of the avenue was open. no one was 
moving at the lodge. the moon was shining, and i could see the great 
house glimmer white through an archway of trees. i walk up it for a 
quarter of a mile or so, until i was at the edge of the drive, where it 
end in a broad, gravel space before the main door. there i stood in 
the shadow and look at the long building, with a full moon shining in 
every window and silver the high stone front. i crouch there for 
some time, and i wonder where i should find the easiest entrance. the 
corner window of the side seem to be the one which was least 
overlooked, and a screen of ivy hung heavy over it. my best chance was 
evidently there. i work my way under the tree to the back of the 
house, and then crept along in the black shadow of the building. a dog 
bark and rattle his chain, but i stood wait until he was quiet, 
and then i stole on once more until i came to the window which i had 
chosen. 

it is astonish how careless they are in the country, in place far 
remove from large towns, where the thought of burglar never enter 
their heads. i call it setting temptation in a poor man’s way when he 
put his hand, meaning no harm, upon a door, and find it swing open 
before him. in this case it was not so bad as that, but the window was 
merely fasten with the ordinary catch, which i open with a push from 
the blade of my knife. i pull up the window as quick as possible, 
and then i thrust the knife through the slit in the shutter and prize 
it open. they were fold shutters, and i shove them before me and 
walk into the room. 

“good evening, sir! you are very welcome!” said a voice. 

i’ve had some start in my life, but never one to come up to that one. 
there, in the opening of the shutters, within reach of my arm, was 
standing a woman with a small coil of wax taper burning in her hand. she 
was tall and straight and slender, with a beautiful white face that 
might have been cut out of clear marble, but her hair and eyes were as 
black as night. she was dress in some sort of white dressing-gown 
which flow down to her feet, and what with this robe and what with her 
face, it seem as if a spirit from above was standing in front of me. 
my knee knock together, and i held on to the shutter with one hand to 
give me support. i should have turn and run away if i had had the 
strength, but i could only just stand and stare at her. 

she soon brought me back to myself once more. 

“don’t be frightened!” said she, and they were strange words for the 
mistress of a house to have to use to a burglar. “i saw you out of my 
bedroom window when you were hiding under those trees, so i slip 
downstairs, and then i heard you at the window. i should have open it 
for you if you had waited, but you manage it yourself just as i came 
up.” 

i still held in my hand the long clasp-knife with which i had open the 
shutter. i was unshaven and grime from a week on the roads. altogether, 
there are few people who would have care to face me alone at one in the 
morning; but this woman, if i had been her lover meeting her by 
appointment, could not have look upon me with a more welcome eye. 
she laid her hand upon my sleeve and drew me into the room. 

“what’s the meaning of this, ma’am? don’t get try any little games 
upon me,” said i, in my roughest way—and i can put it on rough when i 
like. “it’ll be the worse for you if you play me any trick,” i added, 
showing her my knife. 

“i will play you no trick,” said she. “on the contrary, i am your 
friend, and i wish to help you.” 

“excuse me, ma’am, but i find it hard to believe that,” said i. “why 
should you wish to help me?” 

“i have my own reasons,” said she; and then suddenly, with those black 
eyes blazing out of her white face: “it’s because i hate him, hate him, 
hate him! now you understand.” 

i remember what the landlord had told me, and i did understand. i 
look at her ladyship’s face, and i knew that i could trust her. she 
want to revenge herself upon her husband. she want to hit him where 
it would hurt him most—upon the pocket. she hate him so that she would 
even lower her pride to take such a man as me into her confidence if she 
could gain her end by do so. i’ve hate some folk in my time, but i 
don’t think i ever understood what hate was until i saw that woman’s 
face in the light of the taper. 

“you’ll trust me now?” said she, with another coax touch upon my 
sleeve. 

“yes, your ladyship.” 

“you know me, then?” 

“i can guess who you are.” 

“i daresay my wrong are the talk of the county. but what do he care 
for that? he only care for one thing in the whole world, and that you 
can take from him this night. have you a bag?” 

“no, your ladyship.” 

“shut the shutter behind you. then no one can see the light. you are 
quite safe. the servant all sleep in the other wing. i can show you 
where all the most valuable things are. you cannot carry them all, so we 
must pick the best.” 

the room in which i found myself was long and low, with many rug and 
skin scattered about on a polish wood floor. small case stood here 
and there, and the wall were decorate with spear and sword and 
paddles, and other things which find their way into museums. there were 
some queer clothes, too, which had been brought from savage countries, 
and the lady took down a large leather sack-bag from among them. 

“this sleeping-sack will do,” said she. “now come with me and i will 
show you where the medal are.” 

it was like a dream to me to think that this tall, white woman was the 
lady of the house, and that she was lend me a hand to rob her own 
home. i could have burst out laugh at the thought of it, and yet 
there was something in that pale face of hers which stop my laughter 
and turn me cold and serious. she swept on in front of me like a 
spirit, with the green taper in her hand, and i walk behind with my 
sack until we came to a door at the end of this museum. it was locked, 
but the key was in it, and she led me through. 

the room beyond was a small one, hung all round with curtains which had 
pictures on them. it was the hunting of a deer that was paint on it, 
as i remember, and in the flicker of that light you’d have sworn that 
the dogs and the horse were stream round the walls. the only other 
thing in the room was a row of case made of walnut, with brass 
ornaments. they had glass tops, and beneath this glass i saw the long 
lines of those gold medals, some of them as big as a plate and half an 
inch thick, all rest upon red velvet and glowing and gleam in the 
darkness. my finger were just itch to be at them, and i slip my 
knife under the lock of one of the case to wrench it open. 

“wait a moment,” said she, lay her hand upon my arm. “you might do 
better than this.” 

“i am very well satisfied, ma’am,” said i, “and much oblige to your 
ladyship for kind assistance.” 

“you can do better,” she repeated. “would not golden sovereign be worth 
more to you than these things?” 

“why, yes,” said i. “that’s best of all.” 

“well,” said she. “he sleep just above our head. it is but one short 
staircase. there is a tin box with money enough to fill this bag under 
his bed.” 

“how can i get it without waking him?” 

“what matter if he do wake?” she look very hard at me as she spoke. 
“you could keep him from calling out.” 

“no, no, ma’am, i’ll have none of that.” 

“just as you like,” said she. “i thought that you were a stout-hearted 
sort of man by your appearance, but i see that i made a mistake. if you 
are afraid to run the risk of one old man, then of course you cannot 
have the gold which is under his bed. you are the best judge of your own 
business, but i should think that you would do better at some other 
trade.” 

“i’ll not have murder on my conscience.” 

“you could overpower him without harm him. i never said anything of 
murder. the money lie under the bed. but if you are faint-hearted, it 
is better that you should not attempt it.” 

she work upon me so, partly with her scorn and partly with this money 
that she held before my eyes, that i believe i should have yield and 
taken my chance upstairs, had it not been that i saw her eyes following 
the struggle within me in such a crafty, malignant fashion, that it was 
evident she was bent upon making me the tool of her revenge, and that 
she would leave me no choice but to do the old man an injury or to be 
capture by him. she felt sudden that she was give herself away, and 
she change her face to a kindly, friendly smile, but it was too late, 
for i had had my warning. 

“i will not go upstairs,” said i. “i have all i want here.” 

she look her contempt at me, and there never was a face which could 
look it plainer. 

“very good. you can take these medals. i should be glad if you would 
begin at this end. i suppose they will all be the same value when melt 
down, but these are the one which are the rarest, and, therefore, the 
most precious to him. it is not necessary to break the locks. if you 
press that brass knob you will find that there is a secret spring. so! 
take that small one first—it is the very apple of his eye.” 

she had open one of the cases, and the beautiful things all lay 
expose before me. i had my hand upon the one which she had pointed out, 
when sudden a change came over her face, and she held up one finger as 
a warning. “hist!” she whispered. “what is that?” 

far away in the silence of the house we heard a low, dragging, shuffle 
sound, and the distant tread of feet. she closed and fasten the case 
in an instant. 

“it’s my husband!” she whispered. “all right. don’t be alarmed. i’ll 
arrange it. here! quick, behind the tapestry!” 

she pushed me behind the paint curtains upon the wall, my empty 
leather bag still in my hand. then she took her taper and walk quick 
into the room from which we had come. from where i stood i could see her 
through the open door. 

“is that you, robert?” she cried. 

the light of a candle shone through the door of the museum, and the 
shuffle steps came near and nearer. then i saw a face in the 
doorway, a great, heavy face, all lines and creases, with a huge curve 
nose, and a pair of gold glasses fixed across it. he had to throw his 
head back to see through the glasses, and that great nose thrust out in 
front of him like the beak of some sort of fowl. he was a big man, very 
tall and burly, so that in his loose dressing-gown his figure seem to 
fill up the whole doorway. he had a pile of grey, curling hair all round 
his head, but his face was clean-shaven. his mouth was thin and small 
and prim, hidden away under his long, masterful nose. he stood there, 
holding the candle in front of him, and look at his wife with a 
queer, malicious gleam in his eyes. it only need that one look to tell 
me that he was as fond of her as she was of him. 

“how’s this?” he asked. “some new tantrum? what do you mean by wandering 
about the house? why don’t you go to bed?” 

“i could not sleep,” she answered. she spoke languid and wearily. if 
she was an actress once, she had not forgotten her calling. 

“might i suggest,” said he, in the same mock kind of voice, “that a 
good conscience is an excellent aid to sleep?” 

“that cannot be true,” she answered, “for you sleep very well.” 

“i have only one thing in my life to be ashamed of,” said he, and his 
hair bristle up with anger until he look like an old cockatoo. “you 
know best what that is. it is a mistake which has brought its own 
punishment with it.” 

“to me as well as to you. remember that!” 

“you have very little to whine about. it was i who stoop and you who 
rose.” 

“rose!” 

“yes, rose. i suppose you do not deny that it is promotion to exchange 
the music-hall for manner hall. fool that i was ever to take you out 
of your true sphere!” 

“if you think so, why do you not separate?” 

“because private misery is better than public humiliation. because it is 
easy to suffer for a mistake than to own to it. because also i like to 
keep you in my sight, and to know that you cannot go back to him.” 

“you villain! you cowardly villain!” 

“yes, yes, my lady. i know your secret ambition, but it shall never be 
while i live, and if it happen after my death i will at least take care 
that you go to him as a beggar. you and dear edward will never have the 
satisfaction of squander my savings, and you may make up your mind to 
that, my lady. why are those shutter and the window open?” 

“i found the night very close.” 

“it is not safe. how do you know that some tramp may not be outside? are 
you aware that my collection of medal is worth more than any similar 
collection in the world? you have left the door open also. what is there 
to prevent any one from rifling the cases?” 

“i was here.” 

“i know you were. i heard you moving about in the medal room, and that 
was why i came down. what were you doing?” 

“looking at the medals. what else should i be doing?” 

“this curiosity is something new.” he look suspicious at her and 
move on towards the inner room, she walking beside him. 

it was at this moment that i saw something which startle me. i had laid 
my clasp-knife open upon the top of one of the cases, and there it lay 
in full view. she saw it before he did, and with a woman’s cunning she 
held her taper out so that the light of it came between lord mannering’s 
eyes and the knife. then she took it in her left hand and held it 
against her gown out of his sight. he look about from case to case—i 
could have put my hand at one time upon his long nose—but there was 
nothing to show that the medal had been tamper with, and so, still 
snarl and grumbling, he shuffle off into the other room once more. 

and now i have to speak of what i heard rather than of what i saw, but i 
swear to you, as i shall stand some day before my maker, that what i say 
is the truth. 

when they pass into the outer room i saw him lay his candle upon the 
corner of one of the tables, and he sat himself down, but in such a 
position that he was just out of my sight. she move behind him, as i 
could tell from the fact that the light of her taper threw his long, 
lumpy shadow upon the floor in front of him. then he began talk about 
this man whom he call edward, and every word that he said was like a 
blistering drop of vitriol. he spoke low, so that i could not hear it 
all, but from what i heard i should guess that she would as soon have 
been lash with a whip. at first she said some hot words in reply, but 
then she was silent, and he went on and on in that cold, mock voice 
of his, nag and insult and tormenting, until i wonder that she 
could bear to stand there in silence and listen to it. then sudden i 
heard him say in a sharp voice, “come from behind me! leave go of my 
collar! what! would you dare to strike me?” there was a sound like a 
blow, just a soft sort of thud, and then i heard him cry out, “my god, 
it’s blood!” he shuffle with his feet as if he was get up, and then 
i heard another blow, and he cry out, “oh, you she-devil!” and was 
quiet, except for a dripping and splash upon the floor. 

i ran out from behind my curtain at that, and rush into the other 
room, shake all over with the horror of it. the old man had slip 
down in the chair, and his dressing-gown had ruck up until he look 
as if he had a monstrous hump to his back. his head, with the gold 
glasses still fixed on his nose, was loll over upon one side, and his 
little mouth was open just like a dead fish. i could not see where the 
blood was coming from, but i could still hear it drum upon the 
floor. she stood behind him with the candle shining full upon her face. 
her lip were pressed together and her eyes shining, and a touch of 
colour had come into each of her cheeks. it just want that to make her 
the most beautiful woman i had ever seen in my life. 

“you’ve done it now!” said i. 

“yes,” said she, in her quiet way, “i’ve done it now.” 

“what are you go to do?” i asked. “they’ll have you for murder as 
sure as fate.” 

“never fear about me. i have nothing to live for, and it do not 
matter. give me a hand to set him straight in the chair. it is horrible 
to see him like this!” 

i did so, though it turn me cold all over to touch him. some of his 
blood came on my hand and sicken me. 

“now,” said she, “you may as well have the medal as any one else. take 
them and go.” 

“i don’t want them. i only want to get away. i was never mixed up with a 
business like this before.” 

“nonsense!” said she. “you came for the medals, and here they are at 
your mercy. why should you not have them? there is no one to prevent 
you.” 

i held the bag still in my hand. she open the case, and between us we 
threw a hundred or so of the medal into it. they were all from the one 
case, but i could not bring myself to wait for any more. then i made for 
the window, for the very air of this house seem to poison me after 
what i had seen and heard. as i look back, i saw her standing there, 
tall and graceful, with the light in her hand, just as i had seen her 
first. she wave good-bye, and i wave back at her and sprang out into 
the gravel drive. 

i thank god that i can lay my hand upon my heart and say that i have 
never done a murder, but perhaps it would be different if i had been 
able to read that woman’s mind and thoughts. there might have been two 
body in the room instead of one if i could have seen behind that last 
smile of hers. but i thought of nothing but of get safe away, and 
it never enter my head how she might be fix the rope round my neck. 
i had not taken five steps out from the window skirt down the shadow 
of the house in the way that i had come, when i heard a scream that 
might have raise the parish, and then another and another. 

“murder!” she cried. “murder! murder! help!” and her voice rang out in 
the quiet of the night-time and sound over the whole country-side. it 
went through my head, that dreadful cry. in an instant lights began to 
move and window to fly up, not only in the house behind me, but at the 
lodge and in the stable in front. like a frightened rabbit i bolt 
down the drive, but i heard the clang of the gate being shut before i 
could reach it. then i hid my bag of medal under some dry fagots, and i 
try to get away across the park, but some one saw me in the moonlight, 
and presently i had half a dozen of them with dogs upon my heels. i 
crouch down among the brambles, but those dogs were too many for me, 
and i was glad enough when the men came up and prevent me from being 
torn into pieces. they seize me, and drag me back to the room from 
which i had come. 

“is this the man, your ladyship?” ask the oldest of them—the same whom 
i found out afterwards to be the butler. 

she had been bend over the body, with her handkerchief to her eyes, 
and now she turn upon me with the face of a fury. oh, what an actress 
that woman was! 

“yes, yes, it is the very man,” she cried. “oh, you villain, you cruel 
villain, to treat an old man so!” 

there was a man there who seem to be a village constable. he laid his 
hand upon my shoulder. 

“what do you say to that?” said he. 

“it was she who did it,” i cried, point at the woman, whose eyes 
never flinch before mine. 

“come! come! try another!” said the constable, and one of the 
men-servants struck at me with his fist. 

“i tell you that i saw her do it. she stab him twice with a knife. 
she first help me to rob him, and then she murder him.” 

the footman try to strike me again, but she held up her hand. 

“do not hurt him,” said she. “i think that his punishment may safe be 
left to the law.” 

“i’ll see to that, your ladyship,” said the constable. “your ladyship 
actually saw the crime committed, did you not?” 

“yes, yes, i saw it with my own eyes. it was horrible. we heard the 
noise and we came down. my poor husband was in front. the man had one of 
the case open, and was filling a black leather bag which he held in his 
hand. he rush past us, and my husband seize him. there was a 
struggle, and he stab him twice. there you can see the blood upon his 
hands. if i am not mistaken, his knife is still in lord mannering’s 
body.” 

“look at the blood upon her hands!” i cried. 

“she has been holding up his lordship’s head, you lie rascal,” said 
the butler. 

“and here’s the very sack her ladyship spoke of,” said the constable, as 
a groom came in with the one which i had drop in my flight. “and here 
are the medal inside it. that’s good enough for me. we will keep him 
safe here to-night, and to-morrow the inspector and i can take him into 
salisbury.” 

“poor creature,” said the woman. “for my own part, i forgive him any 
injury which he has done me. who know what temptation may have driven 
him to crime? his conscience and the law will give him punishment enough 
without any reproach of mine rendering it more bitter.” 

i could not answer—i tell you, sir, i could not answer, so taken aback 
was i by the assurance of the woman. and so, seeming by my silence to 
agree to all that she had said, i was drag away by the butler and the 
constable into the cellar, in which they lock me for the night. 

there, sir, i have told you the whole story of the event which led up 
to the murder of lord manner by his wife upon the night of september 
the 14th, in the year 1894. perhaps you will put my statement on one 
side as the constable did at manner towers, or the judge afterwards 
at the county assizes. or perhaps you will see that there is the ring of 
truth in what i say, and you will follow it up, and so make your name 
for ever as a man who do not grudge personal trouble where justice is 
to be done. i have only you to look to, sir, and if you will clear my 
name of this false accusation, then i will worship you as one man never 
yet worship another. but if you fail me, then i give you my solemn 
promise that i will rope myself up, this day month, to the bar of my 
window, and from that time on i will come to plague you in your dream 
if ever yet one man was able to come back and to haunt another. what i 
ask you to do is very simple. make inquiry about this woman, watch 
her, learn her past history, find out what use she is making of the 
money which has come to her, and whether there is not a man edward as i 
have stated. if from all this you learn anything which show you her 
real character, or which seem to you to corroborate the story which i 
have told you, then i am sure that i can rely upon your goodness of 
heart to come to the rescue of an innocent man. 


the end 


print by william clowe and sons, limited, london and beccles.

considerable effort, much paperwork and many fee to meet and keep up 
with these requirements. we do not solicit donation in location 
where we have not received written confirmation of compliance. to send 
donation or determine the status of compliance for any particular 
state visit www.gutenberg.org/donate 

while we cannot and do not solicit contribution from states where we 
have not met the solicit requirements, we know of no prohibition 
against accept unsolicited donation from donor in such states who 
approach us with offer to donate. 

international donation are grateful accepted, but we cannot make 
any statement concerning tax treatment of donation received from 
outside the united states. u.s. law alone swamp our small staff. 

please check the project gutenberg web page for current donation 
method and addresses. donation are accept in a number of other 
ways including checks, online payment and credit card donations. to 
donate, please visit: www.gutenberg.org/donate 

section 5. general information about project gutenberg-tm electronic works. 

professor michael s. hart was the originate of the project 
gutenberg-tm concept of a library of electronic works that could be 
freely share with anyone. for forty years, he produce and 
distribute project gutenberg-tm ebook with only a loose network of 
volunteer support. 

project gutenberg-tm ebook are often create from several print 
editions, all of which are confirmed as not protect by copyright in 
the u.s. unless a copyright notice is included. thus, we do not 
necessarily keep ebook in compliance with any particular paper 
edition. 

most people start at our web site which has the main pg search 
facility: www.gutenberg.org 

this web site include information about project gutenberg-tm, 
including how to make donation to the project gutenberg literary 
archive foundation, how to help produce our new ebooks, and how to 
subscribe to our email newsletter to hear about new ebooks.
